Preface

partment of Civil and Mechanical engineering) at the Technical University of Den-
mark in partial fulfilment for the degree Master of Science in Engineering, MSc Eng. 
This thesis accounts for 35 ECTS and was prepared between 28th of August 2023 and 
20th of March 2024 under the direct supervision of Associate Professor Christian An-

ance and assistance during the process. 
 
The Technical University of Denmark, Spring 2024

==PAGE:5==
 
 
iv 
 
Abstract 
This thesis investigates the optimization of Global Warming Potential (GWP) and Net 
Present Cost (NPC) in Single-Family Houses (SFHs) through a detailed examination of 
building element variants and energy-efficiency practices. As the construction sector 
significantly contributes to global CO₂e emissions, identifying strategies to mitigate 
GWP, without escalating life cycle costs, is imperative for sustainable development. 
 
The research in this thesis employs a multi-faceted methodology, including Life Cycle 
Cost (LCC) analysis and Life Cycle Assessment (LCA), grounded in the Danish Building 
Regulations (BR18), and complemented by a comprehensive literature review on sus-
tainable construction practices and regulatory frameworks. Four case buildings, de-
signed to represent a range of gross floor areas (GFA) and surface-to-GFA ratios, 
serve as the basis for assessing various insulation thicknesses, building materials, and 
energy systems' impacts on GWP and NPC. 
 
The findings show that optimal solutions for reducing GWP do not always lead to 
higher NPCs, challenging the prevalent assumption of an inverse relationship be-
tween environmental and economic performance. Particularly, the research high-
lights the significance of optimising building geometry and insulation thicknesses to 
achieve substantial reductions in GWP. Furthermore, the concept of Cost of Con-
served Energy (CCE) was adapted to develop a novel approach for the Cost of Con-
served Carbon (CCC) analysis, offering a framework for balancing GWP reduction 
with financial feasibility. 
 
This study contributes to the field by providing actionable insights and guidelines for 
architects, engineers, and policymakers aiming to design and construct low-GWP 
SFHs without incurring prohibitive costs. It advocates for a more nuanced under-
standing of the trade-offs between environmental and economic objectives in sus-
tainable building practices. Additionally, the thesis calls for an update in building reg-
ulations to incorporate limit values and regulations that promote actual sustainable 
decisions, reflecting the urgent need for climate-responsive construction practices. 
 
In conclusion, this thesis underscores the possibility of achieving significant reduc-
tions in GWP for SFHs through informed design decisions and material selection, 
thereby supporting the transition towards more sustainable and economically viable 
residential buildings.

Introduction  
1 
 
 
 
 
1. Introduction 
In this chapter the background is explained, the objectives and hypotheses are de-
fined, and the structure of the report is outlined. 
 
1.1. Background 
Our planet is experiencing a climate crisis with increasing temperatures and rising 
sea levels (NASA/GISS, 2022), more extreme weather events and a looming ecosys-
tem collapse (Calvin et al., 2023). Buildings contribute significantly to global CO₂e 
emissions and are responsible for roughly 40 % of emissions globally, when consider-
ing building materials and operating emissions (Architecture 2030, n.d.). Further-
more, the global building stock is expected to increase by 75 % from 2020 to 2050 
(International Energy Agency, 2022). 
 
In the light of the current uncertainties faced by the world, researching ways to lower 
the Global Warming Potential (GWP) in single-family houses (SFH) without signifi-
cantly increasing life cycle costs (LCC) is crucial. 
 
The Danish Building Regulations (BR18) has implemented regulations mandating 
that the GWP of all new buildings should be documented though life cycle assessment 
(LCA). BR18 also sets a limit value of 12 kg CO₂e per m² per year (kg CO₂e/m²/year) 
for larger buildings (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, n.d.) and the maximum GWP limit is 
expected to gradually decrease to 7.5 kg CO₂e/m²/year for all buildings in 2029 (So-
cial- og Boligstyrelsen et al., n.d.). A report from BUILD (at Aalborg University) rec-
ommends lowering the limit value to 9.43 kg CO₂e/m²/year for SFHs in 2025 to com-
ply with Danish political targets (Tozan, Olsen, et al., 2023). 
 
According to the Earth-shot project Reduction Roadmap (see chapter 1.4.4), the GWP 
from new buildings should be reduced by 96 % in 2029 to have above a 50 % chance 
to stay within the planetary boundary and 1.5 C° global warming (EFFEKT Architects 
et al., 2023). As such, the GWP from new buildings should decrease to 
0.4 kg CO₂e/m²/year in 2029, if we keep building at the same rate as today. The up-
dated 
Reduction 
Roadmap 
2.0 
proposes 
an 
average 
limit 
value 
of 
5.8 kg CO₂e/m²/year in 2025 and suggests that SFHs should have a limit value of 
4.5 kg CO₂e/m²/year in 2025 (EFFEKT Architects et al., 2023). 
 
Because of the Worlds uncertain economic scene, with high interest rates and infla-
tion, and ongoing effects from the pandemic and Russia’s invasion of Ukraine (United 
Nations - Department of Economic and Social Affairs, 2023), it is important not to 
burden homeowners with higher LCC when making the transition to more sustaina-
ble SFHs. 
 
 
==PAGE:11==
 
Introduction  
2 
 
 
 
 
1.2. Objectives 
This thesis aims to investigate and develop methodologies for effectively optimising 
Single-Family House (SFH) practices to achieve reductions in Global Warming Poten-
tial (GWP) and Net Present Cost (NPC). Additionally, this project seeks to identify 
strategies to make low-GWP SFH an economically feasible alternative to traditional 
"business as usual" approaches. 
 
The main objective of this report is to investigate the feasibility of designing a low-
GWP SFH that maintains an equal or lower NPC compared to a reference case reflect-
ing conventional practices. 
 
The report addresses the main objective through four sub-objectives: 
• 
Identifying the areas of SFH that have the greatest influence on GWP and NPC 
• 
Assessing the extent to which GWP can be optimised while maintaining the same or 
lower NPC 
• 
Developing a method for Cost of Conserved Carbon in a building context 
• 
Analysing the potential for optimising SFH to align with the Safe Operating Space 
for GWP and evaluating its impact on NPC 
 
1.3. Thesis Structure 
The structure of this thesis is based on the IMRaD structure. Furthermore, a section 
called “Guidelines for optimising LCA and LCC in SFH” is included before the conclu-
sion. An overview of the structure is shown in Figure 1. 
 
 
Figure 1 Overview of the thesis structure. 
 
Chapter 1 is an introduction to this thesis and explains the background, objectives, 
and hypothesis for this thesis. The introduction also includes a literature review that 
surveys the existing literature concerning LCA, LCC, operational energy use, absolute 
sustainability, and the interfaces in between.

findings of this thesis.

Introduction

1.4. Literature Review 
The following section reviews the current knowledge and research on sustainability, 
LCA, LCC, operational energy use, and absolute sustainability in the context of the 
Danish construction industry with a focus on SFHs. 
 
1.4.1. The Danish Building Regulation 
The Danish Building Regulations (BR18) is a set of regulations from the Ministry of 
Social Affairs, Housing and Senior Citizens providing administrative and technical 
provisions for new and existing buildings in Denmark. For this master’s thesis the fo-
cus will mainly be on chapter 11 which concerns energy use and climate impact. 
 
BR18 § 250 to § 266 set technical provisions for the energy use of new buildings and 
their parts, such as windows, walls, roofs, floors, and heating system (Social- og Bolig-
styrelsen, n.d.). First and foremost, it stipulates that buildings should be designed, 
constructed, and maintained to avoid unnecessary energy consumption. § 257 and § 
258 provides minimum requirements for heat and energy loss of the building enve-
lope as well as windows and doors. § 251 to 256, 259, and 263 to 265 mandates max-
imum energy use of new buildings for accommodation (called the energy frame) and 
additional requirements such as minimum airtightness, overall transmission loss, and 
the use of renewables, that also needs to be met, when evaluating the energy perfor-
mance of new buildings. The maximum energy frame for new buildings for accom-
modation is calculated as 30 kWh/m² per year plus 1,000 kWh per year divided by 
the heated gross floor area (GFA): 
30
𝑘𝑊ℎ
𝑚2 · 𝑦𝑒𝑎𝑟+
1,000 𝑘𝑊ℎ
𝑦𝑒𝑎𝑟
𝐺𝐹𝐴 
 
 
This second part of the equation considers that smaller buildings use more energy 
per GFA, e.g., buildings of 100, 200, and 300 m² will have an energy frame of 40.0, 
35.0, and 33.3 kWh/m²/year, respectively. 
When calculating the energy use of buildings, the energy use is weighted according to 
the type of energy supply with the use of primary energy factors according to § 252. 
The primary energy factors are as follows: 
• 
Electricity: 
 
1.90 
• 
District heating: 
0.85 
• 
Others: 
 
1.00 
 
In the 2023, regulation regarding LCA of new buildings was added to BR18 (§ 297 
and § 298). § 297 subsection 1 mandates that an LCA documenting the GWP must be 
performed on all new buildings, if they are covered by the regulations mandating en-
ergy frame calculations (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, n.d.). 
§ 297 subsection 2 through 8 describe the methodology. The LCA should use a func-
tional unit (FU) of kg CO₂e per m² reference floor area (RFA) per year over a 50-year 
reference period. 
 
 
==PAGE:14==
 
Introduction  
5 
 
 
 
 
The following modules should be included in the LCA: 
• 
A1-A3 
Production 
• 
B4 
Replacement 
• 
B6 
Operational energy use 
• 
C3-C4 
End of life (EoL) 
• 
D 
Benefits and loads beyond the system boundary 
For EoL modules C3 and C4, a minimum of one of the modules should be included in 
the LCA, but if both are declared then both should be included. Module D is not part of 
the overall LCA result but most the reported separately. 
For the operational energy use, the heating and electricity data from the energy frame 
calculation is used. The heating and electricity data is given without the primary en-
ergy factors. 
As of now there are no requirements for documenting any other impact categories 
than GWP, which should be calculated according to DS/EN15978:2012. 
Input quantities are estimated from construction drawings and all quantities are in-
cluded in the calculation, except fasteners, sealants, local membranes, moisture barri-
ers in foundations, handrails, footboards, and mouldings. Some simplifications are 
made to the quantities according to appendix 2 table 6 in BR18. 
A generic dataset for impacts from the most common materials is provided by BR18 
in appendix 2 table 7. The generic dataset is based on reviewed data from the ÖKO-
BAUDAT Platform. The generic data only follows the EN15804+A1 standard. 
Emission factors for operational energy use (electricity, district heating, and gas) are 
also provided by BR18 appendix 2 table 8. 
§ 298 also provides a limit value of 12,0 kg CO₂e/m²/year for all buildings over 
1,000 m² to comply with. 
 
EPD Standards and BR18 
The EN15804 standard was updated in July 2019 with amendment EN15804+A2, 
which means that 13 environmental impact categories are now required in +A2 com-
pared to 7 in +A1 (One Click LCA, 2023). This means that the two standards in princi-
ple cannot be combined when doing LCA calculations. 
When doing LCA according to BR18 it is however allowed to use the two standards 
together, since only GWP is assessed. When using the +A2 standard GWP is divided 
into GWP-fossil, -biogenic, -luluc and -total. When using +A2 data in LCAs according 
to BR18, the GWP-total values should be used. However, the underlying method in 
the two standards changed from CML in +A1 to ILCD in +A2, which means the two 
GWP values are not identical (Stapel et al., 2022). This often results in slightly higher 
impacts when using the +A2 standard and an average increase of around 3-5 % 
should be expected when comparing +A2 to +A1 (Stapel et al., 2022). 
 
1.4.2. Global Warming Potential and Single-Family Houses in Denmark 
In 2021, BUILD (at Aalborg University) updated a report looking at the GWP of 60 
buildings, of which 10 were SFHs. The report found that the total GWP for SFH ranged 
from 7.3 to 11.7 kg CO₂e/m²/year and had a mean GWP of 9.8 kg CO₂e/m²/year (To-
zan et al., 2021). A more recent study from BU½½ILD looked at 39 SFHs and found 
that the mean total GWP was higher at 10.4 kg CO₂e/m²/year and had wider range of 
5.6 to 13.2 kg CO₂e/m²/year (Tozan, Nielsen, et al., 2023). In terms of operational en-
==PAGE:15==
 
Introduction  
6 
 
 
 
 
ergy use, the studies found that it accounted for 29 and 27 % of the total GWP, respec-
tively. In the first study the 5 cases with the lowest embodied GWP had a mean em-
bodied GWP of 5.8 kg CO₂e/m²/year and a mean operational GWP of 3.2 
kg CO₂e/m²/year, while the 5 cases with the highest embodied GWP had a mean em-
bodied GWP of 8.2 kg CO₂e/m²/year and a mean operational GWP of 2.4 
kg CO₂e/m²/year (Tozan et al., 2021). This suggest that there could be a correlation 
between high embodied GWP and low operational GWP and vice versa, however this 
correlation does not carry over to the total GWP. 
In the second study 10 % of the 39 cases would not be able to comply with the cur-
rent limit value of 12.0 kg CO₂e/m²/year if it applied to SFH, and that in some cases it 
is possible to achieve a 50 % reduction in GWP with existing methods (Tozan, Niel-
sen, et al., 2023). The study found no correlation between embodied and operational 
GWP but found a strong correlation between low operational GWP and geothermal 
and heat pumps as heating system, and a correlation between buildings with two sto-
ries and low embodied GWP, although the latter have a limited sample size (n = 3). 
The combination of a geothermal heating system and two storeys results in the low-
est embodied and operational GWP. When looking at the effect of PV panels on opera-
tional GWP the study found no correlation. Lastly the study looked at the embodied 
GWP of building elements and found that ground slab, roof and external walls had the 
highest contributions, but that there is a big variance between cases. 
 
As part of the project “Boligbyggeri fra 4 til 1 planet” BUILD published a report show-
casing best practice cases in terms of GWP (Garnow et al., 2023). The report includes 
nine SFH with a total GWP ranging from 3.85 to 8.91 kg CO₂e/m²/year, showing that 
significant reductions in GWP is already possible today (compaed to the two above 
studies). The report also shows the GWP per person per year (kg CO₂e/person/year) 
and the nine SFH cases ranges from 90 to 485 kg CO₂e/person/year. Similar to Tozan, 
Nielsen, et al. (2023) the report also concludes that ground deck, roofs and external 
walls has the most significant contributions to GWP. The reports shows that opera-
tional GWP accounts for 20 % of total GWP for SFH, which is around 2/3 of the two 
above studies, suggesting that further energy optimisations could be necessary to 
achieve lower GWP. The report also includes a “construction library” that shows im-
pacts for the major building elements for each case. The library shows that for SFH 
screw foundations, lightweight ground slabs (wood or steel), and wooden external 
walls and roofs with biogenic insulation are generally the best options. 
 
Gross Floor Area 
In 2022 the average SFH in Denmark had a gross floor area (GFA) of 213 m², which is 
an increase of 75 % since 1963 (Julie Trolle Boding, 2023). For comparison terraced 
houses and multi storey dwellings increased by 8 and 12 % respectively in the same 
period. 
A study in The Journal of Architecture compared different types of residential build-
ings in Finland with the aim of assessing “tiny homes” in the context of GWP, opera-
tional energy use, and land use (Kuittinen et al., 2023). They looked at two different 
tiny homes with a GFA of 22 and 23 m² (incl. loft area) with 4 energy scenarios for 
each. The tiny homes were compared to two references groups, the first consisting of 
==PAGE:16==
 
Introduction  
7 
 
 
 
 
18 SFHs and the second of 3 residential blocks. The GFA of the SFHs group ranged 
from 118 to 229 m² and had up to two storeys. 
The study shows that the way GWP is reported have a significant impact on the po-
tential conclusions. The tiny homes had an average GWP per area of 
25.6 kg CO₂e/m²/year which is significantly higher than the reference SFHs at 
14.1 kg CO₂e/m²/year. 
However, when looking at the GWP per capita the tiny homes performed significantly 
better with an average GWP of 213.5 kg CO₂e/capita/year (ranging from 127.8 to 
363.7 kg CO₂e/capita/year) compared to 723.2 kg CO₂e/capita/year for the reference 
SFHs. The same can be seen when comparing total GWP per year with the tiny homes 
ranging from 255.6 to 727.5 kg CO₂e/ year compared the reference SFHs at 2,170.9 
kg CO₂e/year. 
This suggests that reducing GFA can significantly reduce GWP, however it is im-
portant to note that the tiny homes might be considered more “extreme” cases. It also 
suggests that when evaluating the GWP of buildings, it is important to consider the 
functional unit used, as expressing results per area might lead to an overall higher 
GWP. This is also what Heide, et al. (2023) and Petersen & Hatic (2023) found, which 
they suggested can result in socially unfair limit values and higher absolute impacts 
(see chapter 1.4.4 Limit Values and Absolute Sustainability). 
 
1.4.3. Operational Energy Use 
In SBI 2016:09 from Aalborg University Gram-Hanssen & Hansen looked at the differ-
ence between the measure and the calculated energy use for heating of SFHs and con-
cluded that there is a systemic deviation between the two values, which means that 
the more energy efficient SFHs use significantly more energy than the calculated en-
ergy frame suggests. SFHs with an A2020 energy label on average used 110 % more 
energy for heating than calculated (Gram-Hanssen & Hansen, 2016). Since data for 
operational energy comes from the calculated energy frame when doing LCA accord-
ing to BR18 (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, n.d.), there is a risk of a “performance gap”, 
where the GWP from operational energy is significantly lower than what would have 
been the case if the operational energy use were based on measured energy. BUILD 
Rapport 2022:37, which looks at the GWP potential of renovation versus demolition 
with new build, tackles this “performance gap” by including an uncertainty chapter, 
where the GWP from operational energy is multiplied with a factor corresponding to 
the average deviation found in SBI 2016:09 (Eberhardt et al., 2022). 
 
Gaarder et al. (2023) and Sohn et al. (2017) looked at the optimum insulation thick-
ness in terms of GWP, and Yuan et al. (2017) looked at the optimum insulation thick-
ness in terms of cost. All three studies found that there is an optimum relationship be-
tween the reduction in operational GWP/cost and the increase in embodied 
GWP/cost (Gaarder et al., 2023; Sohn et al., 2017; Yuan et al., 2017). Figure 2 illus-
trates this phenomenon. All three studies were based on static energy calculations, 
with Yuan et al. and Gaarder et al. using heat degree days (HDD), and Sohn et al., us-
ing the Danish energy frame software Be10 (v7). 
 
==PAGE:17==
 
Introduction  
8 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 2 Total life cycle impact curve relating building insulation and lifetime heat requirement (Sohn et 
al., 2017). 
 
Gaarder et al. (2023) looked at 12 different scenarios, with scenario 3.B being the 
most reminiscent of Danish conditions and concluded that 300 mm insulation was the 
optimal thickness with the specific environmental data used and a resolution of 50 
mm and that further insulation would lead to a higher overall GWP. Sohn et al. (2017) 
found that the insulation quantities corresponding to the energy frame requirements 
introduced in 2015 (present requirements are identical) are within the optimal range 
for total GWP, but that further insulation could result in higher overall GWP. Howev-
er, Sohn et al. (2017) did not consider the end-of-life phase (EoL) of the insulation 
material, which means, that the optimal insulation amount might be even lower as the 
inclusion of the EoL phases would increase  the embodied GWP. 
 
New Emission Factors from 2025 
Artelia A/S was tasked by SBST to prepare a report with new emission factors for op-
erational energy use for the years 2025 to 2075, which are going to form the basis for 
new emission factors in BR18 from 2025 (Sørensen et al., 2023).

When comparing the new emission factors from Artelia A/S with the present emis-
sion factors in BR18, the new emission factors are significantly lower than the pre-
sent emission factors. The average emission factor has decrease by 33 % for electrici-
ty, 73 % for district heating, and 40 % for gas when looking at the period 2025 to 
2040. When considering that operational energy use on average accounts for 20 to 
29 % of GWP in SFHs (Garnow et al., 2023; Tozan et al., 2021; Tozan, Nielsen, et al., 
==PAGE:18==
 
Introduction  
9 
 
 
 
 
2023) and considering the potential performance gap, these new emission factors are 
going to have a significant impact on the total GWP from SFHs. 
 
1.4.4. Limit Values and Absolute Sustainability 
In 2023 the planetary boundaries (PB) framework was updated and found that six of 
the nine PB was transgressed, and that Earth are no longer inside of the Safe Operat-
ing Space (SoS) for humanity. The PB for climate change have also been transgressed. 
The atmospheric CO₂ concentration is well above the PB and the radiative forcing is 
in the high-risk zone (Richardson et al., 2023). 
 
Tozan, Nielsen et al. (2023) looked at the need for a limit value for SFHs in Denmark 
and concluded that a limit value is needed no later than 2025 to initiate the necessary 
reduction in GWP. The study found that it is possible to reduce GWP by more than 
50 % with current technology and practices, but that there is no motivation to reduce 
GWP when no appropriate limit value has been implemented (Tozan, Nielsen, et al., 
2023).  
 
BUILD at AAU was tasked by SBST to prepare the analytical basis for updated limit 
values for in the period 2025-2027 and concluded that to meet the political target of 
ensuring that 1/3 of new buildings are performing better than currently, the new lim-
it value should be 9.90 kg CO₂e/m²/year for all building (Tozan, Olsen, et al., 2023). If 
the limit value is differentiated based on building category, the report recommends a 
limit value of 9.43 kg CO₂e/m²/year for SFHs.  
 
In 2022 the project “Reduction Roadmap” was published. The project from EFFEKT, 
MOE (Artelia Group), and CEBRA attempts to translate the Paris Agreement and the 
PB for Climate Change into industry specific reduction target for new Danish con-
struction projects (Hill-Hansen et al., 2022). The project found that to stay within 1.5 
°C of warming, new Danish housing projects must reach an average of 0.4 
kg CO₂e/m²/year in total GWP. This target must be reaches in 2029 to have a 50 % 
likelihood of staying within 1.5 °C of warming. The reports states that if the construc-
tion rate is reduced to 25 % of the current rate, the target for total GWP goes up to 1.7 
kg CO₂e/m²/year. The project proposes a limit value for SFHs of 4.5 kg CO₂e/m²/year 
and an average limit value of 5.8 kg CO₂e/m²/year (EFFEKT Architects et al., 2023). 
 
Petersen & Hatic (2023) looked at the way limit values for LCA are setup in Denmark. 
They concluded that using limit values expressed as kg CO₂e/m²/year is socially un-
fair, as building larger SFHs makes it easier to comply the regulations. Since smaller 
buildings uses more material per area (but not in absolute terms), smaller SFH might 
not even be able to comply with future limit values. They looked at 10 case SFHs and 
found that “the bigger house, the easier it is to comply with the 'per square meter’ re-
quirement but the bigger the house, the higher the absolute environmental impact” 
and that the 5 smallest cases were not able to comply with a limit value of 10.5 
kg CO₂e/m²/year even though they had the smallest absolute GWP (Petersen & Hatic, 
2023). Figure 3 displays the results of their calculations. 
 
==PAGE:19==
 
Introduction  
10 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 3 Graph by Petersen & Hatic (2023) showcasing the correlation between GFA, GWP per area and 
absolute GWP. 
 
Petersen & Hatic (2023) proposes that limit values should take this correlation into 
consideration by multiplying the limit values with a factor 
𝐴𝑠𝑡𝑎𝑛𝑑𝑎𝑟𝑑 [𝑚2]
𝐴𝑎𝑐𝑡𝑢𝑎𝑙 ℎ𝑜𝑢𝑠𝑒 [𝑚2] which 
would effectively set an absolute limit value that is expressed per area. 
 
A study by Brejnrod et al. (2017) looked at the absolute environmental performance 
of two SFHs in Denmark - a standard house and an upcycled house (Brejnrod et al., 
2017). The study allocated the earths carrying capacity (CC) to building related ser-
vices. This was done by first allocating the CC to a per capita basis using an egalitarian 
approach, and then allocating the per capita CC to a dwelling service basis using eco-
nomic value. The resulted in a GWP CC for a dwelling service of 287 kg CO₂e/year to 
stay below a global temperature increase of 2 °C (based on IPCC target). To limit radi-
ative forcing to 1 W/m² (precautionary target) the CC is 152 kg CO₂e/year (Brejnrod 
et al., 2017). 
The study looked at the 12 PBs and found that four of them was exceeded by both 
case buildings. When looking at a 50-year reference period and with operational en-
ergy included, the standard house exceeded the CC for GWP (IPCC target) by a factor 
8.3 and the upcycled house by a factor 5.9. The only scenario that did not exceed the 
CC for GWP (IPCC target) was the upcycled house with a 120-year reference period 
and without the operational energy included. The precautionary target was exceeded 
in all scenarios (Brejnrod et al., 2017). The study presented four paths for achieving 
absolute sustainability, which are shown in Table 2 for GWP (IPCC target).

Introduction  
11 
 
 
 
 
 
Another way of allocating a CC to buildings is presented in a study by Heide et al. 
(2023), which bases the allocation on the function of the building and a sufficientari-
an approach. The allocation is based on a sharing principle called “Fulfilment of Hu-
man Needs” (FHN) which is based on the average (economical) consumption in the 
“most sustainable” countries that meet the criteria of a human development index 
>0.7, an ecological footprint <3 gha/capita/year, and a climate change impact <5 tons 
CO₂e/capita/year (Heide et al., 2023). The allocation method results in a CC for GWP 
(IPCC target) of 153.4 kg CO₂e/resident/year. To be able to compare this to Brejnrod 
et al. (2017) the CC should be multiplied with 2.6 residents (which was used by 
Brejnrod et al. (2017)). This results in a CC of 398.8 kg CO₂e/year which is 39 % 
higher than Brejnrod et al. (2017). 
Another study by BUILD, NTNU and SWECO compared the allocation methods for ac-
quired rights (in this case “Reduction Roadmap”) and sufficientarianism. In this study 
the sufficiency scenario is based on the Decent Living Scenario (DLS). The DLS con-
siders how much energy is needed per capita to meet basic needs and concludes that 
dwellings necessitate 18.6 % of the energy consumption per capita, and this percent-
age is then transferred to the CC for GWP per capita in the study. 
The study found that the CC was 2.9 times bigger at 1.16 kg CO₂e/m²/year compared 
to 0.40 kg CO₂e/m²/year in Reduction Roadmap, when using the sufficiency alloca-
tion method compared to acquired rights (Hansen, Hoxha, et al., 2023). 
 
To summarise the different current and prospective limit values as well as ways of 
accessing and allocating the Earths CC for GWP, all above values are shown in Table 3. 
Values are displayed as GWP pr. area pr. year, GWP pr. year, and GWP pr. capita. Since 
most sources only display the value in one of the three ways, all values have been 
converted using an GFA of 213 m² and 2.6 capita per dwelling.

1.4.5. Life Cycle Cost 
Petrovic et al. (2021) did LCA and LCC calculations of a SFH in Sweden, and found no  
full correlation (Petrović et al., 2021). Their results show that when looking at a 50-
year reference period, the production and construction phase is the biggest contribu-
tor to both GWP and LCC at around 55 % of the GWP and 80 % of the LCC. The 
maintenance and replacement phase accounted for around 15 % in both GWP and 
LCC. The biggest difference was in the operational energy, where the phase accounted 
for almost 20 % of the GWP, but less than 5 % of the LCC. When they looked at the dif-
ferent materials, they found that materials like wood, CLT, and cellulose insulation 
had a small share of the GWP in the production phase relative to their share of the 
cost in the production phase. Materials like concrete and PVs had a large share of the 
GWP relative to their share of the cost (Petrović et al., 2021). 
 
Moschetti et al. (2017) analysed a SFH, where they combined LCA and LCC to look at 
the GWP and Net Present Cost (NPC) of different energy efficiency measures for ren-
ovation of the SFH (Moschetti & Brattebø, 2017). They found that in terms of GWP a 
scenario with hydronic air-to-water heat pump, PV system, and solar thermal system 
==PAGE:22==
 
Introduction  
13 
 
 
 
 
resulted in the lowest GWP, while a scenario with all electric heating and no renewa-
ble technology resulted in the highest GWP. However, when looking at the NPC they 
found the opposite to be true and showed a correlation between higher NPC and low-
er GWP (R² = 0.94). They also showed that the electricity mix has a significant impact 
on the GWP of the different scenarios. When they change the electricity mix from the 
Nordel electricity mix (base case) to the Norwegian electricity mix (with lower GWP 
pr. kWh compared to base case), the GWP from operational energy decrease so much 
that the all-electric heating scenario ended up as the best performing scenario in 
terms of GWP. When they changed the electricity mix to the European electricity mix 
(with higher GWP pr. kWh compared to base case) the results from the base case got 
exaggerated (Moschetti & Brattebø, 2017). 
 
A study from Petersen & Svendsen (2012) presents a simplified economic optimisa-
tion method for designing new low-energy buildings. It focuses on the Cost of Con-
served Energy (CCE) model for evaluating energy-saving measures in building design, 
using the energy frame to set maximum energy use requirements. The study uses a 
case to illustrate the method's application in optimising building designs, emphasis-
ing the importance of economic efficiency in building components and services for 
energy conservation. The approach aims to establish a qualified estimate of economi-
cally optimal solutions, serving as a starting point for detailed optimisation in the it-
erative building design process (Petersen & Svendsen, 2012). 
 
1.4.6. Levelized Cost of Conserved Carbon 
Friedmann et al (2020) introduces the concept “Levelized Cost of Carbon Abatement” 
(LCCA) as a more refined methodology for evaluating technologies and policies for 
carbon abatement. LCCA assesses how much CO₂e reduction can be achieved by a 
specific investment or policy, factoring in relevant geographical and asset-specific 
variables. This approach calculates the cost per ton of CO₂e reduced, aiming to pro-
vide a clearer understanding of the actual climate costs and benefits of different poli-
cies and measures (Friedmann et al., 2020). 
Previous methodologies often failed to account for the specific contexts that influence 
the true total cost of a policy and its actual impact on emissions, which can vary wide-
ly based on geography, infrastructure, timing, and other factors. LCCA addresses 
these gaps by including specific and local CO₂e reductions in all estimations and ap-
plying standard financial metrics for a more accurate comparison of costs, e.g. dis-
count rates (Friedmann et al., 2020). 
The LCCA methodology is shown to be crucial for understanding the value of policies 
and investments in terms of achieving greenhouse gas reduction goals and avoiding 
ineffective or costly approaches. While acknowledging that carbon abatement costs 
are just one of many considerations in formulating climate policy, the paper argues 
that LCCA analysis can lead to more efficient and effective GHG reduction strategies, 
helping to avoid wasteful investments (Friedmann et al., 2020). 
 
A list of various LCCA values for different carbon abatement measures is shown in 
Table 4. It is important to keep in mind, that the LCCA for all the technologies shown 
will vary from case to case and by country to country, which is evident from the large 
spread from lower to upper LCCA. All these technologies are also improving over

time. Because of this, these values should be seen as approximations of the cost of 
carbon abatement at the time of writing.

1.5. Hypothesis 
This chapter presents the hypotheses formulated for the thesis. The hypothesis is di-
vided into four different hypotheses: 
1) Correlation between GWP and NPC 
2) Optimal solutions for low GWP and NPC 
3) Optimal insulation thickness 
4) Ability to build within the planetary boundary 
 
Hypothesis 1: Negative correlation between GWP and NPC 
A significant negative correlation exists between Global Warming Potential (GWP) 
and Net Present Cost (NPC), when looking at optimal solutions (the pareto front).  
Rationale: This hypothesis is premised on the understanding that measures to reduce 
GWP often would involve advanced, unconventional, more expensive technologies or 
materials, which may lead to higher upfront or maintenance costs. This inefficiency is 
expected to behave exponentially, resulting in a rising cost for each subsequent re-
duction in GWP. 
 
Hypothesis 2: Optimal solutions for low GWP and NPC 
There are optimal design solutions that achieve relatively low GWP without incurring 
high NPC, before the point of diminishing returns.  
Rationale: This hypothesis suggests the existence of ‘sweet spot’ solutions where en-
vironmental and economic efficiencies are maximised. These would not be the solu-
tions with the absolute lowest GWP and/or NPC, but due the expected exponential 
negative correlation between GWP and NPC, the solutions before the point of dimin-
==PAGE:24==
 
Introduction  
15 
 
 
 
 
ishing returns would strike an optimal balance between GWP and NPC. Adversely, 
non-optimal solutions with both high GWP and NPC are also expected to exist. These 
solutions would simply always be irrelevant solutions (when only considering GWP 
and NPC). 
 
Hypothesis 3: Optimal insulation thickness 
When considering embodied and operational impacts an optimal insulation thickness 
(in building envelopes) can be found, striking the right balance between embodied 
and operation impacts, which results in the lowest possible total impact for that spe-
cific variant of building envelope. 
Rationale: Since heat loss does not scale linearly with insulation thickness but embod-
ied impacts more of less do, a point must exist where the embodied impacts become 
so large, that it is the dominant factor of the total impact and an increase in insulation 
would results in an increase in total impacts. The opposite point where the opera-
tional impacts become the dominant factor would also exist. This would be true for 
both GWP and NPC and due to the expected exponential negative correlation between 
GWP and NPC, the optimal thicknesses would likely not be the same for GWP and 
NPC. 
 
Hypothesis 4: Ability to build within the planetary boundary 
It is possible to build within the PB for GWP with the current technologies existing 
today. It will however require compromises in terms of NPC, Gross Floor Area (GFA), 
material choices, and possibly compromises in areas that are not directly related to 
the building. 
Rationale: By choosing the materials with the lowest possible GWP, the optimising in-
sulations thicknesses, and significantly lowering GFA, a single-family house can be 
built within the PB for GWP. If this is not enough to stay within the PB, other 
measures can be used, such as utilising renewable energy in building operation or 
material production, or other allocation methods for accessing the PB can be ex-
plored.

2.1. Case Buildings 
To support the investigation of how different decisions impact results, such as Gross 
Floor Area (GFA) and ration of surface area to GFA (surface-to-GFA ratio), four case 
buildings were designed. These four case buildings were modelled in 3D using the 
software Autodesk Revit 2023. 
 
The four different case buildings are as following: 
• 
Case 1: Reference building - a typical Danish tract house[1] with the average GFA 
• 
Case 2: Low surface area building with the average GFA 
• 
Case 3: Low surface area building with 25 % reduced GFA 
• 
Case 4: Small building with 50 % reduced GFA 
 
The selection these of four case buildings was strategically made to encompass a range 
of GFA and surface-to-GFA ratios. Case 1 was modelled as a reference building for a 
standard single-family house (SFH). Multiple floor plans from various tract house con-
tractors were used for inspiration and the GFA adjusted to fit the average GFA of 
213 m². Case 2 was modelled as a low surface area alternative to case 1 (with the same 
GFA), to showcase the impact of surface area, while case 3 and 4 was modelled as 
smaller alternatives to showcase the impact of GFA. 
 
The four case buildings have the same internal room height of 2400 mm and a 15-
degree sloped roof. All cases included two bathrooms, a utility room, a depot, a master 
bedroom with a walk-in closet, as well as either separate or combined kitchen-dining 
area and living room. Case 1 and 2 have three bedrooms (excluding master bedroom), 
while case 3 and 4 have two bedrooms. All cases include a home office, except case 4. 
The windows in all bedrooms are sized to achieve a window-to-floor-area ratio of 
10 % (minimum requirement in BR18 § 379), while the windows in the kitchen-dining 
areas and living rooms are sized for a ratio of 15 %. The ratio of window area to floor 
area are calculated according to BR18 chapter 18 and the associated guideline (John-
sen & Lumbye, 2019) and an overall correction factor (including glass to frame ratio) 
of 0.65 have been used. Most of the windows in the living spaces have a constant 
height of 1400 mm, while some (only in the kitchen-dining areas and living rooms) 
have a height of 2110 mm (floor to ceiling). 
The windows in non-living space (bathrooms, corridors etc.) have a size of 600x600 
mm. 
 
Table 5 shows the GFA, number of storeys and surface-to-GFA ratio for the four case 
buildings. The quantities for each case building can be found in appendix B Case Build-
ing Quantities shows.

2.2. Life Cycle Assessment 
Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) is a method used for assessing the environmental im-
pacts of products and systems during their life cycle. In this thesis, the LCAs are per-
formed according § 297 and 298 in BR18, which is based on DS/EN15978:2012 
(Dansk standard, 2012; Social- og Boligstyrelsen, n.d.). The only documented impact 
category in this thesis, is Global Warming Potential (GWP). 
The software LCAbyg 2023 (version 5.3.1.0), which is a free LCA tool developed by 
BUILD (Aalborg University), is used in tandem with Microsoft Excel to perform the 
LCAs in this study. 
All results are shown in kg CO₂e or a derivative thereof (e.g. kg CO₂e/m²/year or 
kg CO₂e/year), unless otherwise stated. If ton CO₂e is used, then it is metric tons. 
 
Goal and Scope Definition 
The studies in this thesis are comparative case studies, which aims to compare the 
GWP of various building elements as well as different case buildings and scenarios. 
The results obtained will be used to develop methods for optimising the GWP of sin-
gle-family houses (SFH). Overall, the aims of the LCAs conducted in this thesis are to: 
• 
Quantify and compare the GWP of various building elements, case buildings, and 
 
scenarios 
• 
Use obtained data to evaluate the cost of reducing GWP in SFHs 
 
Functional unit 
The functional unit will change depending on which products and systems are as-
sessed in the following sections of the thesis and will be highlighted in the relevant 
method section. When assessing the results obtained in this thesis, the various im-
pacts will only be compared to impacts with the same functional unit. 
 
Reference period 
The reference period used is 50 years, as mandated by BR18 chapter 11 (Social- og 
Boligstyrelsen, n.d.). The reference period of 50 years is kept constant in all assess-
ments made in this thesis. The service life of buildings materials and components are 
based on the “service life tables” published by BUILD (Haugbølle et al., 2021), as 
mandated by BR18 chapter 11. 
 
System boundaries 
The assessments include the life cycle modules as shown in Figure 9. The included 
modules are chosen to comply with BR18 2023 (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, n.d.). 
==PAGE:32==
 
Methodology  
23 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 9 Building life cycle modules (based on EN 15978) and the defined system boundaries. 
Light grey modules are included, and dark grey modules are not included. 
 
This means that the production phase (A1-A3), the use phase (B4 and B6), as well as 
End of Life (EoL) phase (C3 and/or C4) are included in the assessments. For the EoL 
phase, one of either C3 or C4 must be included according to BR18 (Social- og Bolig-
styrelsen, n.d.), but if both are provided in the dataset used, then both must be in-
cluded. 
The remaining modules are not included in the assessments, as it is not part of the 
BR18 requirements. However, the impacts of these modules are not assumed to be 
insignificant - especially as GWP from the included modules becomes smaller. BUILD 
Report 2023:14 and 2023:23 (Balouktsi & Birgisdottir, 2023; Kanafani et al., 2023) 
found that the remaining modules accounts for around 3 kg CO₂e/m²/year (median 
value). Table 6 shows the lower quartile, median and upper quartile values for mod-
ules A4, A5, B1, B2, B3, C1, and C2 according to BUILD Report 2023:14 and 2023:23.

Life Cycle Inventory 
The LCAs will be made with the software LCAbyg (developed by BUILD at Aalborg 
University) and Microsoft Excel. LCAbyg is a free tool for calculating LCA of buildings 
and is used to calculate the GWP various building components and elements. Mi-
crosoft Excel is used to combine the data obtained in the variant study as well as data 
from energy calculations into whole building LCAs. 
 
Energy consumption 
The energy consumption in module B6 (Operational energy use) is based on data 
from the energy frame calculations (calculated with Be18). 
See chapter 2.4 Operational Energy Use for more detailed methodology regarding en-
ergy consumption. 
==PAGE:33==
 
Methodology  
24 
 
 
 
 
GWP from operational energy use is based on the updated emission factors for 2025 
(Sørensen et al., 2023). 
 
Life Cycle Impact Assessment 
The Life Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA) phase of the LCA is used for quantifying and 
evaluating the environmental impacts attributable to the project under investigation. 
Given the focus on GWP, this LCIA specifically targets the greenhouse gas emissions 
across the life cycle stages, from raw material extraction to end-of-life processes. The 
assessment adheres to the standards outlined in EN 15804+A1 and EN 15804+A2, 
which provide the frame for evaluating the environmental performance of construc-
tion products. 
For this assessment, two distinct environmental data sources were utilised, corre-
sponding to the EN 15804+A1 and EN 15804+A2 amendments. The +A1 amendment 
relies on the Centre of Environmental Science (CML) methodology and references the 
Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) 2007 guidelines for calculating 
GWP. Conversely, the +A2 amendment adopts the International Reference Life Cycle 
Data System (ILCD) methodology, with GWP calculations based on the updated IPCC 
2013 guidelines.  
The GWP impact category measures the potential of emitted greenhouse gases to trap 
heat in the atmosphere, contributing to global warming. This is expressed in terms of 
carbon dioxide equivalents (CO₂e) over a specified time horizon, allowing the com-
parison of the warming impacts of different gases. Using the environmental data 
based on EN 15804+A1 and EN 15804+A2, the greenhouse gas emissions were con-
verted into CO₂e based on their GWP values. Given the singular focus on GWP, no 
normalisation or weighting have been used in this project. 
The transition from CML to ILCD methodologies and from IPCC 2007 to IPCC 2013 
guidelines represents an evolution in scientific understanding and consensus on cli-
mate change impacts. It is crucial to acknowledge the potential variations in GWP 
outcomes due to these differences, emphasising the importance of transparent meth-
odology and clear communication of the chosen standards and guidelines. The stand-
ard used for each material used can be seen in appendix C Environmental Data. 
Although this thesis primarily focuses on GWP, it acknowledges the importance of a 
broader range of environmental impacts. The inclusion of additional impact catego-
ries is recommended for future research to provide a more comprehensive under-
standing of environmental implications associated with single-family houses. 
 
Environmental data 
The environmental data used in the assessments is based on generic data from 
GenDK (which is a dataset based on ÖKOBAUDAT, but validated by BUILD), generic 
data from ÖKOBAUDAT (Federal Ministry for Housing Urban Development and Build-
ing, 2024), Danish industry EPDs from EPD Danmark (Teknologisk Institut, 2024), as 
well as product specific EPDs. 
 
To obtain results that are not based on specific products and as representative as 
possible for the Danish building industry, the environmental data is chosen according 
to the hierarchy shown in Figure 10. 
 
==PAGE:34==
 
Methodology  
25 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 10 Hierarchy showing the order in which environmental data types are preferred. 
The top is the most preferred and the bottom is the least. 
 
During the project period of this thesis, BUILD published a report containing new ge-
neric LCA data based on EPDs from the Danish building industry (Kragh & Birgisdot-
tir, 2023). Unfortunately, the report was published too late in the process, to be able 
to incorporate the new generic dataset, as this would have meant redoing a very large 
share of the work already done. If this data had been incorporated into the report, it 
would have been the most preferred environmental data type in this project. 
Looking at how the incorporation of the data could have affected the findings of the 
thesis, there is no clear pattern of the new generic values generally having higher or 
lower GWP than the GenDK data based on ÖKOBAUDAT. Some data have higher im-
pacts, while some data have lower impacts, but a lot of the new generic data do not 
have a direct counterpart from ÖKOBAUDAT. This means that the new generic data 
might results in slightly more specific data overall and are generally more representa-
tive for the Danish building industry. 
 
The IDs for environmental data used can be seen in appendix C Environmental Data. 
 
Most datasets used in the assessments are based on the European standard EN 
15804+A1, however in some cases data based on EN 15804+A2 is used if +A1 data 
was not available. Data from the two standards are not directly comparable since 
more impact categories has been introduced in the +A2 standard, however the im-
pacts for GWP in +A1 and GWP-total in +A2 are expressed in the same units and im-
pacts are within 3-5 % on average (with +A2 usually having higher impacts) (Stapel 
et al., 2022). This difference is considered acceptable for this thesis, however it 
should be noted that the GWP are generally going to be slightly higher, when the shift 
the +A2 data are finalised.

2.3. Life Cycle Cost 
The Life Cycle Cost (LCC) calculations are calculations of the expected total monetary 
costs during a building’s life. The LCC calculations include costs for procurements, 
maintenance, and replacement of building parts, as well as costs for energy consump-
tion. All assumptions for the LCCs can be seen in appendix A.2. 
 
All prices are in DKK and excl. VAT. 
To include Danish VAT, 25 % must be added to all prices. The inclusion of VAT does 
not alter any of the findings of this thesis. 
At the time of writing 1 DKK = 0.13 EUR = 0.15 USD. 
 
Net Present Cost 
When assessing the total economic costs, all future costs are discounted using a pro-
jected discount rate before all the costs are summed up. The sum of the upfront costs 
and the discounted future costs is the Net Present Cost (NPC), which illustrates how 
much money one should put aside today to be able to cover the costs for the entire 
reference period. NPC is calculated using the formula: 
𝑁𝑃𝐶(𝜏) = 𝐶𝑖∑
𝐶𝑓,𝑖
(1 + 𝑟)𝑖+ ∑𝐶𝐸𝑂𝐿,𝜏(𝑗) −𝑉𝑓,𝜏(𝑗)
(1 + 𝑟)𝜏
𝑗
𝜏
𝑖=1
 
where 𝑁𝑃𝐶(𝜏) is the net present cost of the calculation period 𝜏, 𝐶𝑖 is the initial in-
vestment cost, 𝐶𝑓,𝑖 is the future cost at the year 𝑖 (e.g. maintenance, energy costs, re-
placement), 𝐶𝐸𝑂𝐿,𝜏(𝑗) is the End-of-Life (EoL) costs of the component 𝑗, 𝑉𝑓,𝜏(𝑗) is the 
final value of the component 𝑗 at EoL, and 𝑟 is the real discount rate (Moschetti & 
Brattebø, 2017). 
 
Reference period 
The LCC calculations are performed for a 50-year reference period, as this is assumed 
to be a good compromise between accommodating long-term considerations and tak-
ing future uncertainties into account (VÆRDIBYG & Realdania, 2019). This also al-
lows for better interaction with the LCA results, since the reference period is the 
same. 
The service life of buildings materials and components are based on the “service life 
tables” published by BUILD (Haugbølle et al., 2021). This means the service life is the 
same as used in the LCA. 
 
System boundaries 
The LCC calculations follow the system boundaries from the LCAs, however construc-
tion/installation, maintenance and demolition are also included in the LCC. 
The calculations include all costs from the life cycle of all major building components 
and systems (e.g. roof, external walls, internal walls, windows, HVAC systems, PV 
panels) during the reference period. 
The calculations also include costs for energy consumption during the reference peri-
od. The data for energy consumption is the same as the data used for LCAs. 
The LCC calculations does not include costs for building plot, site development and 
other site related costs, fees for consultants or other fees not directly associated with 
==PAGE:37==
 
Methodology  
28 
 
 
 
 
the building components or systems. Costs for operational water use and sewage are 
also not included in the calculations. 
 
Tool and Data 
The LCC calculations are carried out using LCCbyg, which is a free software developed 
by BUILD at Aalborg University. LCCbyg contains generic data (e.g. maintenance and 
replacement costs, service lifetimes, energy prices, discount rates, etc.), which have 
been used for the LCC calculations. 
 
Projected discount rate 
A flat projected discount rate of 3 % has been used for all calculations. This is the 
same model used for DGNB Denmark and is recommended by VÆRDIBYG when as-
sessing LCC for energy conserving measures (VÆRDIBYG & Realdania, 2019). 
 
Price data 
Prices used for the calculations is mostly based on prices from Molie Prisdata, which 
is a tool used in the Danish construction industry to calculate price estimates. 
Labour prices are all based on data from Molio Prisdata, while material prices are ei-
ther based on Molio Prisdata or prices found in online shops. 
Price data for energy are based on prices available in LCCbyg, with district heating 
costing 0.52 DKK/kWh, electricity costing 1.77 DKK/kWh. When electricity is used for 
heating, all electricity use above 4000 kWh gets a price deduction of approximately 
0.89 DKK/kWh, resulting in an electricity price of 0.88 DKK/kWh (Patursson & 
Gregersen, 2024). 
Data for maintenance is based on generic values available in LCCbyg. The mainte-
nance costs are calculated as a percentage of the procurement costs, which is applied 
yearly. The maintenance costs ranged from 0.5 and 5 % depending on the compo-
nent/system type and the material type. 
Replacement and End of Life (EoL) costs are also calculated as a percentage of the 
procurement costs. The percentages used are the standard values used in LCCbyg 
which are 25 % for EoL and 125 % for replacement (25 % for EoL and 100 % for pro-
curement of new building component/system). 
All prices are based on data from Q1 2023. If the data used are older than Q1 2023, 
then the data are adjusted according to the price development index for construction 
from Molio (MOLIO, 2023). 
 
 
 
==PAGE:38==
 
Methodology  
29 
 
 
 
 
2.4. Operational Energy Use 
As part of the assessments of GWP and NPC, the operational energy use of the case 
buildings is needed. 
For the whole building calculations, the energy frame is calculated according to SBi-
213. For the optimisation of insulation thicknesses, the data from the energy frame 
calculations are used to generate a U-value dependent heat degree days (HDD) func-
tion for each case buildings. 
 
2.4.1. Energy Frame 
The energy frame calculations are made according to chapter 11 of The Danish Build-
ing Regulations (BR18) using the software Be18, which is a software developed by 
BUILD at Aalborg University (formerly known as SBi) specifically to perform energy 
frame calculations. 
 
The four case buildings are designed to comply with the standard requirements and 
limits for energy frame and overall transmission losses (transmission loss frame) are 
shown in Table 7. 
 
Case

[m²]

[kWh/m²/year]

39.3 
20.8 
Table 7 Limit values for energy frame and overall transmission losses of for the four case buildings. 
 
The case buildings are generally designed to comply with the energy frame and the 
overall transmissions losses. However, in some cases when assessing the optimal in-
sulation thicknesses, the limit value for overall transmission loss cannot be complied 
with. This is considered to be alright, as the higher transmission loss results in lower 
GWP and/or NPC. 
In cases where optimising the insulation thicknesses results in the building not com-
plying with the energy frame, PV-panels are added to the energy frame calculation in 
order to offset the results and comply with the energy frame. 
As part of the assessments in this thesis, direct electric heating is also, however with 
the case buildings used in the assessments, it is not practically possible to comply 
with the energy frame without significantly altering the prerequisites for the case 
buildings. This is mainly due to § 265 subsection 2 in BR18 stating that no more than 
25 kWh/m²/year of primary energy from renewable energy sources can be included 
in the energy frame calculations. In these cases, the number of PV-panels are adjusted 
to deliver the maximum allowed amount of primary energy from renewable energy 
sources. When assessing the results related to direct electric heating, it is important 
to consider the fact that these cases cannot comply with the energy frame with the 
current regulations. 
 
The perquisites for energy frame calculations can be seen in appendix D Prerequisites 
for Energy Frame Calculations. 
 
==PAGE:39==
 
Methodology  
30 
 
 
 
 
2.4.2. Heat Degree Days 
To be able to optimise the insulation thicknesses for the various building elements 
assessed, a U-value dependent HDD function was developed for each case building. 
To develop the functions, seven energy frame calculations were made for each case 
building (using the software Be18), with varying U-values for the external wall, roof, 
and ground deck, ranging from a U-value of 0.05 to 0.35 W/(m²·K). These calculations 
were made with the perquisites shown in appendix D and according to chapter 11 of 
The Danish Building Regulations (BR18). However, to make the HDD resemble more 
realistic scenarios, the setpoint for indoor temperature was increased from 20 to 22 
°C. 
The calculations did not necessarily comply with the relevant limit value for the ener-
gy frame, as this exercise was exclusively used to generate the function for the HDD 
and compliance at this stage is irrelevant. 
Data for heating requirements from each calculation was then used to assess the 
share of heating energy delivered by the heating system and subsequently used to 
calculate the average amount of degrees heated by the heating system for each 
month, using the formula: 
Δ𝑇ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡,𝑠𝑦𝑠𝑡𝑒𝑚= 𝑞ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡,𝑠𝑦𝑠𝑡𝑒𝑚
𝑞ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡,𝑡𝑜𝑡𝑎𝑙
· Δ𝑇 
where Δ𝑇ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡,𝑠𝑦𝑠𝑡𝑒𝑚 [𝐾] is the temperature difference heated by the heating system, 
Δ𝑇 [𝐾] is the average temperature difference between the indoor and outdoor tem-
perature, 𝑞ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡,𝑠𝑦𝑠𝑡𝑒𝑚 [𝑀𝑊ℎ] is the amount of heat delivered by the heating system, 
and 𝑞ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡,𝑡𝑜𝑡𝑎𝑙 [𝑀𝑊ℎ] is the total heat delivered to the building including solar and in-
ternal heat gains. 
The HDD for an entire year can then be calculated using the formula: 
𝐻𝐷𝐷𝑦𝑒𝑎𝑟= ∑Δ𝑇ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡,𝑠𝑦𝑠𝑡𝑒𝑚,𝑛· 𝑑𝑛

𝑛=1
 
where 𝐻𝐷𝐷𝑦𝑒𝑎𝑟 [𝐾· 𝑑] is the HDD for a whole year, Δ𝑇ℎ𝑒𝑎𝑡,𝑠𝑦𝑠𝑡𝑒𝑚 [𝐾] is the average 
temperature difference heated by the heating system for each given month, and 𝑑 [𝑑] 
is the number of days in a given month. 
The above calculations were made for each U-value variant of each of the case build-
ings and was done for both elements towards air (external wall and roof) and ele-
ments towards ground (ground deck). Lastly a continuous function showing the rela-
tionship between HDD and U-value was made for each case building. These functions 
can be seen in Table 8, Figure 13, and Figure 13. 
It is important to note that the energy frame calculations were made with homogene-
ous U-values across the different elements, and that after optimisation of the insula-
tion thicknesses the U-values might not be homogeneous, which results in an uncer-
tainty in the HDD. Through a small sample of new HDD calculations with inhomoge-
neous U-values, the uncertainty is estimated to be around 5 %.

2.5. Building Element Library 
To compare the GWP and NPC of various building components and elements, a vari-
ant study was performed to form a building element library. The methodologies for 
assessing the GWP and NPC are described in chapter 2.2 Life Cycle Assessment and 
2.3 Life Cycle Cost, respectively.  
 
The assessment begins on a building component level (or a sub-element level) with 
comparisons of different surface and insulation materials as well as materials for the 
structural systems. These findings are then used to make comparisons on a building 
element level, where the entire assembly of various elements are compared. 
 
At the component level the following component types are compared: 
• 
Insulation (external wall, internal wall, roof, floor separation and ground deck) 
• 
External surface components (external wall and roof) 
• 
Internal surface components (external wall, internal wall, ceiling, and floors). 
• 
Structural components (external wall, internal wall, roof, floor separation and 
 
ground deck) 
At the element level the following element types are compared: 
• 
External wall 
• 
Internal wall 
• 
Roof 
• 
Floor separation 
• 
Ground deck 
 
The functional unit depends on the component or element type being compared. At 
the element level the functional unit is the always defined as 1 m² building envelope 
with a U-value of 0.10 W/(m²·K). At the component level the function units used are: 
• 
Insulation, external: 1 m² insulation with a thickness corresponding to a
 
U-value of 0.10 W/(m²·K) 
• 
Insulation, internal wall: 1 m² insulation with a thickness of 100 mm 
• 
Insulation, floor separation: 1 m² insulation with a thickness of 200 mm 
• 
External and internal surfaces: 1 m² surface material, with the necessary 
 
subassembly and surface treatment 
• 
Structural: 1 m² structural system with the ability to store or attach insulation 
 
with a thickness corresponding to a U-value of 0.10 W/(m²·K) 
 
When comparing the different components at the component level, it should be noted 
that not all components are compatible, e.g. some insulation types can be used 
directly on the ground in a ground deck, while other types require a structural system 
to be used in a ground deck. This is however taken into consideration when the 
comparisons are made on the element level.

To limit the complexity and the number of possible combinations, it has been chosen 
only to consider two types of heating systems in this thesis: hydronic and electric ra-
diant floor heating (FH). The pipes (hydronic) and mats (electric) have been consid-
ered as part of the floor surface components. 
 
Based on the components assessed at the component level, various element variants 
are constructed by combining different types of components. Before constructing the 
element variants, a simple preliminary filtering of the components was performed, to 
eliminate non-optimal component variants. The preliminary filtering was done by 
eliminating variants that had simultaneously higher GWP and NPC than other vari-
ants, while still maintaining enough variants to ensure compatibility with all the other 
component types (e.g. maintaining both soft and rigid insulation variants for use with 
different structural and surface variants).

•

It should be noted, at not all possible combinations of components have been con-
structed and assessed, as there are simple to many compatibility restrictions to au-
tomate the process and to many possible combinations to do manually (e.g. external 
walls have more than 1,300,000 possible combinations, if ignoring compatibility re-
strictions). This means that there are probably some combinations that results in 
some optimal variants, which have not been assess in this thesis, however, this is 
deemed acceptable, as it is simply not practically possibly to assess all combinations. 
 
For each element type, a reference type is also assessed. The reference type repre-
sents one of the most commonly used variants in standard Danish single-family hous-
es (SFH). The build-up of the reference variants can be seen in Table 10. The refer-
ence types have all been considered with a U-value of 0.10 W/(m²·K) to be directly 
comparable with the other element types. However, the reference external wall is al-
so considered with a higher U-value of 0.13 W/(m²·K) when performing the whole 
building assessments, as it is seen as a more realistic U-value for a standard Danish 
SFH.

2.6. Cost of Conserved Carbon 
“Cost of Conserved Carbon” (CCC) is an adaptation of the concept “Cost of Conserved 
Energy” (CCE) from the referenced article on component based economical optimisa-
tion for low-energy buildings (Petersen & Svendsen, 2012). The adapted CCC is tai-
lored to measure the economic efficiency of GWP reduction measures in building de-
sign and in this study specifically SFHs. 
 
The CCC model adapts the economic optimisation framework of the CCE model to fo-
cus on GWP reduction in the context of SFH. This approach provides a systematic way 
to evaluate and optimise building components or elements for GWP reduction, while 
considering economic feasibility, thus contributing to the development of sustainable 
(in terms of GWP) and cost-effective housing solutions. 
 
The CCC methodology maintains a component- or element-based perspective, as-
sessing individual measures (e.g. insulation, surface materials, wall assembly, etc.) for 
their GWP reduction potential relative to their economic life cycle cost. In this thesis 
the CCC concept is only used on the element level. 
 
For each building element type, the element variant with the lowest NPC is defined as 
the baseline. There is no specific GWP reduction target, as the goal simple is as low as 
possible. 
 
The potential GWP reduction for each element variant is calculated as the difference 
between the baseline (for that element type) and the assessed element variant. 
 
The NPC is used as the economic component of CCC. The cost of achieving each level 
of GWP reduction is calculated as the difference in NPC divided with the potential 
GWP reduction: 
𝐶𝐶𝐶= 𝑁𝑃𝐶𝑟𝑒𝑓𝑒𝑟𝑒𝑛𝑐𝑒−𝑁𝑃𝐶𝑣𝑎𝑟𝑖𝑎𝑛𝑡
𝐺𝑊𝑃𝑟𝑒𝑓𝑒𝑟𝑒𝑛𝑐𝑒−𝐺𝑊𝑃𝑣𝑎𝑟𝑖𝑎𝑛𝑡
 
 
Similar to the CCE method, the CCC approach involves an adapted four-step optimisa-
tion process: 
1) A reference is chosen (as stated above) 
2) Variants with negative CCC are rejected 
3) Non-optimal variants are rejected (only Pareto front is retained) 
4) Continuous function is generated based on the marginal CCC elements 
 
The optimisation process is illustrated in Figure 14. 
 
==PAGE:45==
 
Methodology  
36 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 14 Four-step optimisation process for “Cost of Conserved Carbon”. 
The figure is an adapted version of fig. 3 in Petersen & Svendsen, 2012. The arrows do not show all re-
jected variants in step 3. 
 
The CCC concept is applied in two rounds: firstly, to all element types in the building 
element library, whereas only the optimal variants (marginal CCC elements) are used 
for further assessment. Secondly, after optimising the insulation thicknesses of the 
optimal variants found in the first round, the CCC concept is applied again find new 
case specific optimal variants. 
 
After the first round of applying the CCC concept, the following number of optimal el-
ement variants are remaining: 
•

9 
•

4 
•

5 
• 
Floor separation: 
16 (10 hydronic FH, 6 electric FH) 
• 
Ground deck:  
11 (7 hydronic FH, 4 electric FH) 
 
The build-up of the optimal element variants found in the first round of CCC can be 
seen in appendix E.2 Optimal Building Elements and their GWP.

Methodology  
37 
 
 
 
 
2.7. Optimisation of Insulation Thicknesses 
To ensure the optimal relationship between embodied and operational impacts (in 
terms of both GWP and NPC), the insulation thicknesses can be optimised to achieve 
the lowest GWP or NPC. This optimisation is done to all optimal element variants 
found in the first round of applying the CCC concept and for each case building, except 
internal walls and floor separations, as these do not have a heat loss to optimise for. 
The build-up of the optimal element variants found in the first round of CCC can be 
seen in appendix E.2 Optimal Building Elements and their GWP. 
 
To optimise the insulation thicknesses, the GWP and NPC of each variant is divided 
into a fixed value and a variable value. The fixed value represents the part of the ele-
ments whose thickness cannot be adjusted - this is the internal and external surfaces 
and usually the structural system, however structural systems like wood framing will 
be at least partly variable. The variable value represents the part of the element 
whose thickness can be adjusted to significantly alter the thermal resistance of the el-
ement. This is usually limited to the insulation, however in elements with e.g. wood 
framing the structural system will also have to be at least partly variable, as men-
tioned above. For the variable NPC value, only the NPC of the materials are variable, 
while the NPC for wages is keep constant, as the NPC for wages are assumed to be 
relatively constant regardless of thickness. 
When the fixed and variable values are known, all U-values for thicknesses between 0 
and 1000 mm are calculated in 5 mm increments. These U-values and used together 
with the relevant quantities (e.g. external wall area) and U-value dependent HDD 
function (see chapter 2.4.2 Heat Degree Days) to calculated the heat loss for each 
thickness. It is important to use U-values dependent HDD, as the large variance in in-
sulation thickness results in significant differences in heat loss, which can then signif-
icantly affect the HDD. Afterwards the GWP and NPC are calculated for each thick-
ness, considering the material embodied GWP and NPC as well as the GWP and NPC 
from operational energy use. The reference period is 50 years. 
Lastly the optimal thicknesses in terms of both GWP and NPC are found for each ele-
ment variant and used as new variants in further assessments (see concept in Figure 
15). 
==PAGE:47==
 
Methodology  
38 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 15 Example of how the relationship between embodied, operation, and total GWP affects the op-
timal insulation thickness in terms of GWP. 
A similar graph can be made for NPC with the Y-axis showing the NPC in DKK. 
 
A balanced variant is also constructed for each element variant, simply by taking the 
average insulation thickness of the optimal thickness for GWP and the optimal thick-
ness for NPC. These three new variants are found for each element type and for each 
case building as well as for each type of heat supply (district heating, heat pump, and 
direct electric heating). Table 11 shows the number of variants being used for further 
assessment for each case building.

2.8. Whole Building Assessment 
In this section, the results from the previous assessments are combined to calculate 
the GWP and NPC of the case buildings in their entirety. 
 
The functional unit used for the LCA in this section is “GWP of an entire building di-
vided by the GFA divided by the reference period.” However, the results of this sec-
tion are generally shown with different units, depending on what is being assess. The 
units can either be kg CO₂e/m²/year (GWP/GFA/year), kg CO₂e/year (GWP/year), or 
kg CO₂e (total GWP). 
The functional unit used for the LCC assessments in this section is “NPC of an entire 
building” and the results are generally shown as DKK (total NPC), unless otherwise 
stated. 
 
When choosing which building elements to use in the whole building assessment, on-
ly the optimal variants found in chapter 2.7 Optimisation of Insulation Thicknesses 
are considered. To be able to test all possible solutions a Python script has been writ-
ten. The code for this script can be seen in appendix G Multi-Object Optimisation. 
The GWP and NPC values of all the optimal variants are given to the script along with 
the relevant quantities for each building element type. As all building element vari-
ants are optimal variants, a weighting factor is used to determine how to optimise the 
solutions. To give the best representation of the relationship between GWP and NPC, 
five different weighting factors are used in the script: 1.00/0.00, 0.75/0.25, 0.50/0.50, 
0.25/0.75, and 0.00/1.00 (GWP/NPC). 
The script is setup to find the optimal solutions for all four case buildings by calculat-
ing all possible combinations and return the variants used and the total GWP and NPC 
of the optimal solution for each case. 
 
The script uses the five building element types assessed in the variant study, but also 
includes two variants for windows (wood and wood-alu windows). The number of 
possible combinations for each case building ranged between 1,440 and 93,600, and 
the total number of possible combinations was 383,080 across all case buildings and 
types of heat supply. The number of variants used in the script for each case building 
and heat supply can be seen in Table 11. 
 
As not all building parts of the case buildings are included in the previous assess-
ments, the GWP and NPC values of missing building parts have been calculated 
(method according to chapter 2.2 Life Cycle Assessment and 2.3 Life Cycle Cost) and 
are shown in Table 13. 
The GWP for operational energy use is based on the values shown in chapter 2.2 Life 
Cycle Assessment, and the NPC is based on the values shown in chapter 2.3 Life Cycle 
Cost. The NPCs for operational energy use are 0.33 DKK/kWh for district heating, 
1.25 DKK/kWh for electricity, and 0.62 DKK/kWh for all electricity above 4,000 kWh 
used for heating. The operational energy use was calculated as described in chapter 
2.4 Operational Energy Use.

3. Results and Analysis 
In this chapter presents and analyses the results obtained as part of this thesis. 
It is important to note, that the findings of this chapter are in the context of a single-
family house (SFH), and that other factors such as structural, stabilising, fire, and 
sound properties can significantly change the conclusions drawn from these results. 
 
The results are divided into the following five sections: 
• 
Building Element Library and preliminary CCC 
• 
Optimisation of Insulation Thicknesses 
• 
Cost of Conserved Carbon 
• 
Whole Building Assessment 
• 
Absolute Sustainability 
• 
Sensitivity Analysis 
 
It is important to note that the findings of this chapter are in the context of a single-
family house (SFH), and that other factors such as structural, stabilising, fire, and 
sound properties can significantly change the conclusions drawn from these results. 
It is also important to note that the chosen LCA methodology can significantly impact 
the results, especially for biogenic materials. This phenomenon is discussed in 
chapter 4.2 Attributional and Consequential LCA. 
 
3.1. Building Element Library and preliminary CCC 
The following section presents the findings from the building element library and the 
Cost of Conserved Carbon (CCC) (chapter 2.5 and 2.6). To see all results from the 
building element library see appendix E.1, the results of the optimal element variants 
found in the first round of CCC can be seen in appendix E.2, and the CCC graphs for 
each element type can be seen in appendix E.3. 
 
Cost of Conserved Carbon (CCC) 
The “Cost of Conserved Energy” (CCE) was adapted as described in chapter 2.6 Cost of 
Conserved Carbon to evaluate the economic efficiency of carbon reduction measures. 
The concept was applied to the building elements constructed as described in chapter 
2.5 Building Element Library. 
The results of the first round of applying the CCC concept on the external and internal 
wall variants, can be seen in Figure 17 and Figure 18, respectively. The rest of the var-
iants can be seen in appendix E.3. 
10 % (9 out of 87) of external wall variants were optimal variants on the pareto front, 
while 16 % (4 out of 25) of the internal wall variants were optimal variants. The rest 
of the element types, the percentages were 13, 20, 21, 20, and 21 % for roof, ground 
deck (hydronic), ground deck (electric), floor separation (hydronic), and floor separa-
tion (electric), respectively. 
All the external wall variants have a CCC above 50 DKK/kg CO₂e (or 50,000 DKK/ton 
CO₂e), the internal wall variants a CCC above 25 DKK/kg CO₂e, the ground deck vari-
ants a CCC above 15 DKK/kg CO₂e, the floor separation variants a CCC above 5 
DKK/kg CO₂e, and lastly some roof variants have a CCC around 1.5 DKK/kg CO₂e.
