Optimal Building Element Variants 
Looking qualitatively at the optimal building elements and their build-ups, some pat-
terns emerge. For the external walls, the optimal variants fall within two categories: 
• 
Inner leaf, rigid insulation, and plaster (ETICS) 
• 
Wood framing, straw, and gypsum board 
The first category resembles an ETICS wall and consists of three layers: a structural 
inner leaf (lightweight concrete or CLT) that also acts as the internal surface, a layer 
of rigid insulation, and cement plaster as the external surface. The inner leaf is un-
painted, as paint would increase both GWP and NPC. The rigid insulation layers are 
either graphite EPS, mineral wool or woodfibre, depending on variant. 
The second category consists of wood framing as the structural system, a board mate-
rial as the internal surface (gypsum plaster board or gypsum fibre board), an installa-
tion layer with a PE vapour membrane, and straw as the insulation material between 
the wood framing. The external surface consists of either a rigid woodfibre insulation 
with cement plaster or an external gypsum board, battens, and wood cladding. The 
wood framing can be either traditional wood framing, framing with I-beams or fram-
ing with the double stud method.  
The first category is generally high GWP and low NPC, while the second category 
shows the opposite. 
 
For internal walls, the optimal solutions also fall within two similar categories: 
• 
Massive element 
• 
Framing with clay boards 
The first category simply consists of either a 100 mm lightweight concrete wall or a 
100 mm CLT wall. The walls act as the internal surfaces and is unpainted. The second 
category consists of either wood or steel framing with straw insulation and clay 
boards as internal surfaces. Again, the first category is generally high GW 
P and low NPC, while the second category shows the opposite. 
 
==PAGE:54==
 
Results and Analysis 
 
45 
 
 
 
 
For roofs, almost all optimal variants consist of a traditional triangle wood truss with 
straw insulation, varying board materials as internal surface as well as varying 
external surface materials. Only one variant deviates from this trend, as it consists of 
a lightweight concrete deck with rigid insulation, external membrane, battens, and 
concrete roof tiles. This variant is the variant with the lowest NPC, but the GWP is 
more than double that of the variant with the second lowest NPC. 
 
For ground decks, almost all optimal variants consist of chipboard on rigid insulation 
as the structural system with varying screed layers and floor heating (FH) systems 
(electric and hydronic). Two variants deviate from this trend and consists of 
woodfibre filled wood framing as the structural system, with chipboard and a screed 
layer on top and either electric or hydronic floor heating system. These two variants 
have the lowest GWP, but also the highest NPC. 
 
For floor separations, no inherent patterns are present, as the optimal variants 
consists of a mix of various concrete deck elements, wooden deck elements, wood 
trusses with straw insulation and various board materials, and various FH systems. 
Generally, the variants with concrete deck elements (lightweight concrete and SL 
deck) have the highest GWP and lowest NPC, while the variants with wooden deck 
elements (CLT rib deck) have the lowest GWP and the highest NPC. The variants with 
wood trusses fall in-between (thought with a large range of GWP and NPC). 
 
For windows, only two variants were assessed: wood and wood-alu windows (both 
with 3-panes). The wood variant has a the lowest GWP (almost half the GWP of the 
wood-alu) and around a 25 % higher NPC compared the wood-alu variant. 
 
Generally, for all elements, a negative correlation between GWP and NPC is present. 
This can be seen in Figure 19. For some element types, there are variants that 
decrease either GWP or NPC without significantly increasing the other. This is 
apparent in the three roof variants with the lowest NPC, which shows very little 
increase in NPC (9 percent) and a significant decrease in GWP (79 percent). The 
opposite is true for two of the internal wall variants, where a significant reduction in 
NPC (63 percent) results in a less significant increase in GWP (23 percent). Both 
examples are highlighted in Figure 19. 
 
==PAGE:55==
 
Results and Analysis 
 
46 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 19 GWP and NPC for all optimal variants of all element types. 
Results are displayed pr. area of building element (not pr. area of GFA). To prevent clutter, all variants of 
ground and floor separations with electric FH have been excluded, but they show the same trend. K = 
1,000. 
 
Looking specifically at variants with soft insulation (loose fill, batts, etc.), often the 
optimal insulation variant is straw insulation (In11 in Figure 20), as it is the variant 
with the lowest GWP and almost the lowest NPC. The straw insulation can be re-
placed with lose fill woodfibre insulation (In42) for a small increase in both GWP and 
NPC or with mineral wool batts (In02) for a modest increase in GWP and a small de-
crease in NPC. 
For rigid insulation both standard EPS (In20 and In21), graphite EPS (In17), rigid 
mineral wool (In04), and rigid woodfibre (In45) are all optimal variants, but with a 
clear negative correlation between GWP and NPC (GWP decreases, and NPC increases 
in the stated order). 
Some product specific EPDs have been included in Figure 20 (grey dots) but have not 
been used for further assessments. The included EPDs are Rockwool batts, loose fill 
paper wool, loose fill woodfibre, and sheep’s wool batts/rolls. The EPDs generally all 
have low GWP, however it should be noted that the Rockwool EPDs incorporate green 
certificates (GOs). 
Figure 20 shows the GWP and NPC of different insulation variants in an external wall. 
See appendix E.1 Building Component GWP and NPC for insulation variants in inter-
nal wall, roof, ground deck, and floor separation.

Figure 20 GWP and NPC for 1 m² insulation in an external wall with a U-value of 0.10 W/(m²K).

Overall, it seems that variants with simple build-up (with minimal internal and 
external surfaces) often results in lower NPC values (as is apparent with e.g. the 
ETICS style external walls). These simpler variants incorporate cheaper prefabricated 
elements (SL deck, lightweight concrete, or CLT) and does not include an internal 
surface treatment and uses cheaper options for external surfaces (e.g. cement plaster 
or concrete roof tiles). 
It also seems that using variants with lightweight and biogenic materials results in 
lower GWP and higher NPC. This can be seen in the external wall variants with 
different variants of wood framing, internal walls with wood or steel framing, roofs 
with wood trusses, and floor separations with floor trusses or CLT rib decks. 
The two above findings seem to make materials like CLT a good balanced option for 
external and internal walls, since it is biogenic, lightweight and a prefabricated 
element allowing for a simpler build-up. 
The above is true for most element types, however, ground decks are an exception. It 
seems that using chipboard as a lightweight slab, results in low GWP and low NPC, 
since chipboard has significantly lower GWP and NPC than other options (especially 
concrete). Besides chipboard being a good option for ground decks, it generally seems 
that using thinner screed layers (and thereby thinner FH system) results lower GWP 
but also higher NPC. 
 
Inefficient Building Components 
When assessing the different components during the construction of the building 
element library, some inefficient components were found which had both high GWP 
and high NPC, making them practically irrelevant. Some examples are XPS, PU, wet-
process rigid woodfibre, hemp/flax, and cork insulation. Other examples are shown in 
Table 15. It is important to note, that these inefficient components can be efficient in 
other scenarios and that using non-generic data can alter their GWP and NPC, leading 
to improved efficiency.

Reference Building Elements Variants 
When assessing the reference variants (presented in Table 10), it is apparent that 
none of the variants are optimal variants. The reference variants for internal wall, 
roof and floor separation perform well, while the reference variants for external wall 
and ground deck performs significantly worse than the optimal variants. This can be 
seen in Table 31.

3.2. Optimisation of Insulation Thicknesses 
This section presents the findings from the optimisation of insulation thicknesses. To 
see all results of the optimisation of insulation thicknesses, see appendix F. 
 
The optimal variants discussed in the previous chapter, were used to calculate the op-
timal insulation thickness for each variant, as described in chapter 2.7 Optimisation of 
Insulation Thicknesses. The optimisations were done case specific and for three types 
of heat supplies: District heating (DH), Heat pump (HP), and Direct electric heating 
(EH). 
The optimisation results shows that approx. 90 % of the assessed variants benefits 
from having U-values above 0.10 W/(m²·K) in terms of GWP and NPC. The values that 
do benefit from U-values lower than 0.10 W/(m²·K) are almost exclusively variants 
with EH as heat supply. Looking exclusively at variants with EH, 30 % benefited from 
U-values below 0.10 W/(m²·K). The mean U-values of each element type and heat 
supply can be seen in Table 16.

Table 16 Mean U-values after optimisation. 
(GWP) and (NPC) shows which parameter are used for optimisation. ΔU shows the difference between 
U-values optimised for GWP and NPC. DH = District heating, HP = Heat pump, EH = Direct electric heat-
ing, SD = Standard Deviation. 
 
As seen in Table 16, the variants with EH have the lowest optimal U-values and the 
variants with HP have the highest optimal U-values, while variants with DH falls in 
between. Looking at the different element types, ground deck variants have the high-
est U-values, roof variants the lowest, and external wall variants in between. 
Generally, elements optimised for low NPC results in lower U-values, than elements 
optimised for low GWP. Ground deck variants shows the biggest difference in mean 
U-values for the two optimisation parameters. 
The difference in mean U-values between GWP and NPC optimised variants are be-
tween -0.01 and 0.02 for external wall and roof variant, and 0.04 and 0.08 for ground 
deck variants. This discrepancy is probably due to the fact that most ground deck var-
iants use some form of EPS insulation, which is an insulation type with high GWP and 
low NPC (as is evident from Figure 20), while the other variants use a mix of EPS, 
mineral wool, woodfibre, and straw insulation, which all have different relationships 
between GWP and NPC. Looking at the variants individually, the biggest difference in 
the GWP and NPC optimised U-values is 0.11 for roof variant R17 with HP, with a 
==PAGE:59==
 
Results and Analysis

GWP optimised U-value of 0.28 W/(m²·K) and a NPC optimised U-value of 
0.17 W/(m²·K). 
The optimisation generally leads to lower GWP and/or NPC values, as seen in Figure 
22, suggesting that the optimisation works. Looking at external variant E40 with DH 
as an example, optimising for GWP results 16 % lower GWP and 2 % higher NPC, op-
timising for NPC results in 10 % lower GWP and 1 % lower NPC, and optimising 
50/50 for GWP and NPC results in 14 % lower GWP and 1 % lower NPC. Variant E19 
shows slightly different results with the GWP optimised variant resulting in 3 % low-
er GWP and 4 % lower NPC and the NPC optimised variant resulting in 10 % higher 
GWP and 12 % lower NPC.

tion thickness for each variant is usually within 5 to 15 mm across cases, however for 
DH and HP as heat supply, a few variants differ up to 90 mm, and for EH some vari-
ants differ up to 220 mm, suggest that the EH is more volatile. These bigger variances 
only occur when the insulation thicknesses have been optimised for NPC.

All of the CCC calculations (after the optimisation of insulation thickness) are case 
specific, in contrast to the first round of CCC. This makes it possible to combine the 
CCC calculations and generate a continuous function for CCC of each case building as a 
whole. These functions can be seen in Table 17, Figure 25, and Figure 26. The case 1, 
the functions are calculated for separately for each type of heat supply: district heat-
ing, heat pump, and direct electric heating. For the other case buildings, the functions 
are only calculated with district heating. 
Figure 25 shows the continuous functions for CCC with GWP measured in 
kg CO₂e/m²/year, while Figure 26 shows the continuous functions for CCC with GWP 
measured in kg CO₂e/year. 
The functions are generated by combining the continuous CCC functions for the ex-
ternal wall, internal wall, roof, ground deck, and floor separation. The CCC functions 
for internal wall and floor separation are from the first round of CCC, as no optimisa-
tion of insulation thickness was done with these variants. A case specific “baseline 
GWP”-value was added to each function to make the functions represent the whole 
building GWP. The baseline GWP corresponds to the GWP values of the remaining 
building parts shown in Table 13 (in chapter 2.8) and ranges from 2.13 to 2.62 
kg CO₂e/m²/year depending on case building. 
The CCC functions are validated in chapter 3.4.2.

Table 17 Continuous function for CCC for each case building with district heating with y = CCC 
[DKK/kg CO₂e] and x = GWP (unit as shown in heading). 
The functions are generated from the case specific optimal variants found in the 2nd round of CCC. 
 
The functions show the CCC experience an exponential growth, with lower GWP re-
sulting in exponentially higher NPC. 
When assessing the GWP in kg CO₂e/m²/year, case 4 shows higher CCC-values at the 
same GWP, meaning that it is more expensive to reduce the GWP to the same target, 
while case 2 shows the lowest CCC-values (closely followed by case 3). This is specu-
lated to be due to the more energy efficient designs of case 2 and 3, as they have a 
lower surface-to-GFA ratio, meaning that choosing a more expensive variant will not 
lead to the same increase in NPC as in case 1 or 4. As case 2 and 3 also uses less ener-
gy, the optimal insulation thicknesses are usually lower, resulting in even lower GWP 
and NPC. As an example, if case 4 were to target a GWP of 5.0 kg CO₂e/m²/year, the 
CCC-value would be 6.0, while case 2 would have a CCC-value of 3.0 to hit the same 
target. The GWP for each case building at various CCC-values can be seen in Table 18 
and Table 19. 
However, as is evident from Figure 26 and Table 19, perspective is paramount. When 
changing the GWP parameter from GWP/GFA/year to GWP/year, which better repre-
sents the absolute GWP, the picture changes completely. Now case 4 have the lowest 
CCC-values, followed by case 3, then case 4, and lastly case 1. At a CCC-value of 0.1, 
case 4 has a GWP/year of 801 kg CO₂e/year, while case 1, 2, and 3 needs a CCC of 
32.5, 14.0, and 3.4 respectively, to achieve the same GWP/year.

3.4. Whole Building Assessment 
This section presents the findings from the whole building assessments, which com-
bines all the previous steps to assess how the total GWP and NPC of the case buildings 
are affected. To see all results of the whole building assessments, see appendix H. 
Firstly, the results from the multi-object optimisation will be presented, then the CCC 
functions from chapter 3.3 will be validated, and afterwards the overall findings of 
the whole building assessments will be presented. 
 
All the presented GWP values are calculated with the updated 2025 emission factors 
for operational energy use. This means that the case buildings have lower GWP values 
than most other buildings, which are calculated with 2023 emission factors. A simple 
comparative assessment has been carried out as part of the whole building assess-
ments, and it showed that going from the 2023 to the 2025 emission factors results in 
between 0.2 and 2.7 kg CO₂e/m²/year lower overall GWP, and that on average the 
change resulted in 1.2 kg CO₂e/m²/year lower overall GWP. The greatest difference 
was seen in relation to district heating as heat supply with a drop of 1.7 to 2.7 
kg CO₂e/m²/year, while heat pump and direct electric heating resulted in a drop of 
0.2 to 0.3 and 0.3 to 0.5 kg CO₂e/m²/year, respectively. 
 
3.4.1. Multi-Object Optimisation 
As part of the whole building assessments, a Python script for multi-object optimisa-
tion was written and used to find the optimal combinations of element variants for 
each case with each type of heat supply (12 combinations of cases and heat supplies). 
The optimisation was done for different weightings of GWP and NPC, as described in 
chapter 2.8 Whole Building Assessment, and an example of the Python script can be 
seen in appendix G.1. 
The typical optimal combination of element variants can be seen in Table 20 (typical 
= the combination that occurs most often across the four case buildings). The table is 
divided into two sets of optimal combinations for each weight: One for the hydronic 
heating systems (DH and HP) and one for direct electric heating (EH). To see the op-
timal combinations for each case building and heat supply, see appendix G.2. 
The suffix following the element variant (e.g. _GWP) shows what parameter the insu-
lation thickness has been optimised for.

3.4.4. Building Geometry 
To assess the impact of geometry, in terms of GFA and surface-to-GFA ratio, a set of 
12 variants have been assessed. The set is divided into 3 subsets: a reference set with 
element variants according to Table 10, a low GWP set optimised for the lowest 
possible GWP, and a low NPC set optimised for the lowest possible NPC. Each subset 
includes each case building. The three subsets have been chosen to illustrate the 
effects of GFA and surface area at different levels of GWP and NPC. 
The element variants used for the low GWP subset are according to Table 38 in 
appendix G.2 (variants with 100%  weight on GWP), while the element variants used 
for the low NPC subset are according to Table 37 in appendix G.2 (variants with 
100%  weight on NPC). For the reference subset and low NPC subsets, the heat supply 
is district heating, while the low GWP subset uses heat pump as heat supply. The total 
GWP and NPC of the 12 variants can be seen in Figure 27. 
The four different case buildings are designed to showcase the effects of the chances 
shown below and have the parameters shown in Table 5 in chapter 2.1 Case Build-
ings. 
• 
Case 1: Reference building - a typical Danish tract house with the average GFA 
• 
Case 2: Low surface area building with the average GFA 
• 
Case 3: Low surface area building with 25 % reduced GFA 
• 
Case 4: Small building with 50 % reduced GFA

==PAGE:68==
 
Results and Analysis 
 
59 
 
 
 
 
Reference subset 
Comparing case 1 with case 2 represents a reduction in the surface-to-GFA ratio of 
13 % at the same GFA. This reduction in surface area results in a reduction of 10 % in 
GWP and 12 % in NPC. 
This reduction in both GWP and NPC comes almost without any trade-offs, as the GFA, 
materials and heat supply remains unchanged, and only the architecture is affected 
by the change. If the change in architecture is considered insignificant by the end-
user, then this change from case 1 to case 2 is essentially a “free” reduction in both 
GWP and NPC. 
 
Comparing case 2 with case 3, and case 1 with case 4, represents a reduction in GFA 
of 25 and 50 percent, respectively, at relatively comparable ratios of surface area to 
GFA. 
Looking at case 2 and 3, the 25 % reduction in GFA results in a 22 and 21 % reduction 
in GWP and NPC, respectively. Looking at case 1 and 4, the 50 % reduction in GFA 
results in a 47 and 45 % reduction in GWP and NPC, respectively. 
These to comparisons suggests that the reductions in GWP and NPC scales almost 
linearly with reduction in GFA. The reductions in GWP and NPC are however 
approximately 6 to 16 % lower than the reduction in GFA. 
These reductions in GWP and NPC are however not considered “free” reductions, as a 
reduction in GFA in its nature means having less living area, which can be a significant 
trade-off. Reducing GFA also results in a higher NPC pr. area: going from case 1 to 4, 
the NPC pr. area increases from 15,945 DKK/m² to 17,499 DKK/m². This increase in 
NPC pr. area could potentially result in a monetary loss at a future sale, if a potential 
buyer does not consider a smaller GFA a valid reason for a higher price pr. area. 
 
Low GWP and NPC subsets 
The low GWP and NPC subsets shows the same results when reducing GFA, with 
reductions in GWP and NPC scaling almost linearly with reductions in GFA. The 
reductions in GWP and NPC are, however, slightly lower compared to the reduction in 
GFA (approximately 4 to 16 percent). 
Looking at the effects of reducing surface-to-GFA ratio, the low GWP and NPC subsets 
again shows similar results as the reference subset. However, with slightly higher 
reductions in GWP and slightly lower reductions in NPC for both subsets. 
 
It is important to note that, since these subsets are optimised for either low GWP or 
NPC, the absolute reductions are not as significant (in the optimised parameter) as in 
the reference subset, since the starting point is already relatively low. For 
comparison, the GWP optimised case 1 have a 49 % lower GWP compared to the 
reference case 1, while the NPC optimised case 1 have a 23 % lower NPC compared to 
the reference case 1. 
 
 
==PAGE:69==
 
Results and Analysis 
 
60 
 
 
 
 
3.4.5. Heat Supply 
Three types of heat supplies are used in these assessments: district heating (DH), heat 
pump (HP), and direct electric heating (EH). 
To assess the impact of the three types of heat supply, a set of 15 variants have been 
assessed. Each set consist of 5 variants for each heat supply: the reference case, and 
each of the four cases with parameters optimised for a 50/50 weighting of GWP and 
NPC. The element variants used are according to Table 37, Table 38Table 38, and 
Table 39 in appendix G.2. The total GWP and NPC of the 15 variants can be seen in 
Figure 28. 
 
 
Figure 28 Effect of heat supply on GWP and NPC. 
“Ref, XX” have elements variants according to Table 10, “C#, XX” are the four case buildings with variants 
optimised for a 50/50 weighting of GWP and NPC. GWP and NPC values are total values for the reference 
period and K = 1,000. 
 
Looking at GWP, Figure 28 shows that HP results in the lowest GWP across all vari-
ants, while EH results in the highest GWP and DH lands in between. In terms of NPC, 
DH results in the lowest NPC across all variants, while HP results in the highest NPC 
in all cases except the optimised case 1, where EH results in the highest NPC. 
For the reference case, changing from EH to DH results in a 9 % reduction in GWP, 
and going from DH to HP results in another 6 % reduction. The increase in NPC when 
going from DH to EH is 2 % and another 1 % when going from EH to HP. The same re-
sults are true for the optimised variants of case 2, 3 and 4, however with slightly low-
er absolute reductions in GWP and NPC. 
Investigating the relationship between embodied and operation GWP, the same pat-
terns emerge, as with the total GWP. Generally, EH results in higher embodied GWP. 
This is expected to be due to the higher insulation thicknesses obtained in the chapter 
3.2, since the electric floor heating systems generally have lower GWP than the hy-
dronic counterpart, when looking isolated at the floor heating systems. The HP vari-
ants have the lowest embodied GWP, which is expected to be due to the same reason 
as the high GWP for the EH variants.

61 
 
 
 
 
Looking at the relationship between embodied and operational NPC, a different pat-
tern emerges. The EH variants have the lowest NPC overall, but also the highest oper-
ational NPC, while HP have the highest embodied NPC and DH have the lowest opera-
tional NPC. 
Generally, the choice of heat supply seems to be a choice between a lower GWP in ex-
pense of a higher NPC or a lower NPC in expense of a higher GWP. In all the assessed 
variants, DH performed better than EH in GWP as well as NPC, while HP performed 
better in GWP. This means that EH should never be considered when looking at GWP 
and NPC (at least in these assessed variants). However, EH do have the lowest em-
bodied NPC, and while embodied NPC is not the exact same as procurement cost, it 
does suggest that EH will lead to the lowest procurement cost, which can be positive 
in many scenarios. 
 
Effect of PV-panels 
An assessment was conducted as part of this thesis, investigating the effects of PV-
panels on GWP and NPC. 
All variants with DH and EH shown in Figure 28, as well as the optimised case 1 with 
HP, includes PV-panels. To see the effects of PV-panels on GWP and NPC, the panels 
were removed from the energy frame calculations, as well as the GWP and NPC as-
sessments. 
Excluding the PV-panels from the assessments resulted in an average reduction of 
15 % in embodied GWP and an average increase of 38 % in operational GWP. This re-
sulted in an average reduction of 7 % in total GWP, ranging from a 1 to 13 % reduc-
tion in total GWP. For NPC, the exclusion resulted in an average reduction of 2 % in 
embodied NPC and an average increase of 45 % in operational NPC. This resulted in 
an average increase of 1 % in total NPC, ranging from a 0 to 2 % increase in total NPC. 
In a few cases, the exclusion of PV-panels resulted in a reduction in both GWP and 
NPC, however only a 0.3 % reduction in NPC. 
These results suggest that not installing PV-panels would be a good investment in 
terms of lower GWP, as the increase in NPC is significantly smaller than the reduction 
in GWP. Calculated the Cost of Conserved Carbon (CCC) for the exclusion of PV-panels 
results in CCC-values between -2 and 21 DKK/kg CO₂e, with an average of 11 
DKK/kg CO₂e. 
It is important to note that excluding the PV-panels from the energy frame calcula-
tions, results in the assessed variants not complying with the energy frame require-
ments (EH variants already did not comply as described in chapter 2.4.1 Energy 
Frame). These potential reductions are therefore not possible with the current regu-
lations. It is also important to note that the calculations are done using generic envi-
ronmental data for GWP, and that using product specific EPDs can potentially result 
in higher total GWP when excluding the PV-panels. 
 
 
==PAGE:71==
 
Results and Analysis 
 
62 
 
 
 
 
3.4.6. Building Element Variants 
To assess the impact of material choices (element variants), each case building has 
been assessed with the optimal combination of variants according to Table 37 in 
appendix G.2. Each case is assessed with all five weightings of GWP and NPC as 
described in chapter 2.8. This results in 20 variants plus the reference case, which can 
be seen in Figure 29. 
 
 
Figure 29 Effect of building element variants on GWP and NPC. 
“Reference” have elements variants according to Table 10, “C#” are the four case buildings with all five 
optimal variant combinations shown in appendix G.2. GWP and NPC values are total values for the refer-
ence period and K = 1,000. 
 
Figure 29 shows that all four case buildings show the same negative exponential cor-
relation between GWP and NPC, meaning that the NPC increases exponentially with 
the amount of reduction in GWP. 
Because of this negative exponential correlation, the first reductions in GWP are the 
most economically efficient. Going from the lowest possible NPC variant (rightmost 
variant) to the second lowest NPC variant results in a reduction in GWP of 31 to 35 % 
(depending on case) and only an increase in NPC of 7 to 8 %. This results in a CCC-
value of between 7.7 and 10.2 DKK/kg CO₂e, depending on case. In contrast, going 
from the second lowest GWP variant to the lowest GWP variant, results in an increase 
in NPC of 10 to 17 % (depending on case) and only a GWP reduction of 3 to 6 %. This 
results in a CCC-value of between 268 and 512 DKK/kg CO₂e depending on case, 
which is significantly higher. 
The percentage difference and the CCC between the variants for each case building 
can be seen in Table 22 and Table 23.

3.5. Absolute Sustainability 
In this section the previous findings are combined to assess whether it is possible to 
building within the planetary boundaries (PB) today and whether it is expected to be 
possible to build net-zero SFHs in the year 2045. 
 
3.5.1. Planetary Boundaries 
To investigate the possibility of building within the PB for GWP, a set of 11 variants 
have been assessed. The set consists of the reference case and 10 cherrypicked vari-
ants across the four case buildings. Each case building has been assessed with differ-
ent optimal combinations of element variants according to appendix G.2. The results 
of these assessments can be seen in Figure 30 and an explanation of the variants used 
can be seen in Table 24.  
 
Table 3 in chapter 1.4.4 Limit Values and Absolute Sustainability, shows current and 
prospective limit values as well as values for the Safe Operating Space (SoS) allocated 
to buildings with different allocation methods. 
 
Looking at the limit values for SoS in Table 3, the 2025 limit value from Reduction 
Roadmap 2.0 has the highest limit at 959 kg CO₂e/year. However, it does decrease to 
85 kg CO₂e/year in 2030. The second highest is the sufficiency-based limit from Heide 
et al. (2023) at 399 kg CO₂e/year and the third highest is Brejnrod et al. (2017) limit 
for IPCC < 2 °C at 287 kg CO₂e/year.

Figure 30 shows that the reference case has a GWP of 1,330 kg CO₂e/year, which is 
above all the limit values for SoS and cannot be considered sustainable in terms of 
GWP. It does, however, comply with the current and all prospective limit values not 
based on SoS. 
For case 1, variant “C1, HP (25/75)” have a GWP of 881 kg CO₂e/year, which is below 
the 2025 limit value from Reduction Roadmap 2.0, but significant. This shows that it 
is possible to achieve GWP value below the 2025 limit value from Reduction 
Roadmap 2.0 with the same floor plan, while also achieving a 13 % reduction in NPC 
compared to the reference case. Since all the remaining variants have lower GWP, this 
means that they all are below the 2025 limit value from Reduction Roadmap 2.0. 
For case 2, variant “C2, DH (25/75)” have a GWP of 846 kg CO₂e/year, which is also 
below the 2025 limit value from Reduction Roadmap 2.0. However, this variant 
achieves a 25 % reduction in NPC compared to the reference case. Variant “C2, HP 
(100/0)” have a GWP of 558 kg CO₂e/year, which is 40 % above the sufficiency-based 
SoS value by Heide et al. (2023) and 94 % above the IPCC < 2 °C value by Brejnrod et 
al. (2017). 
For case 3, the two variants “C3, DH (100/0)” and “C3, HP (100/0)” have GWP values 
of 461 and 433 kg CO₂e/year, respectively. This is 16 and 9 % above the sufficiency-
based SoS value as well as 60 and 51 % above the IPCC < 2 °C value. 
The four variants of case 4 have GWP values ranging from 398 to 357 kg CO₂e/year, 
which are all below the sufficiency-based SoS value and between 24 and 39 % above 
the IPCC < 2 °C value. Variant “C4, DH (75/25) - PV” originally needs PV-panels to 
comply with the energy frame regulations. When including the PV-panels, the GWP is 
not below the sufficiency-based SoS value and excluding the panels would result in 
the variant not complying with the energy frame regulations. Despite of this the vari-
==PAGE:75==
 
Results and Analysis 
 
66 
 
 
 
 
ant have been included in Figure 30, as is it the variant with the lowest NPC, which 
have a GWP below the sufficiency-based SoS value. 
 
These results show that, depending on allocation method, it is possible to build SFHs 
within the PB for GWP. It does, however, require a 50 % reduction in GFA (compared 
to the average new SFH in Denmark) as well as material combinations optimised for 
the lowest possible GWP, which results in a significant increase in NPC. Even though 
the NPC is still 27 % below the reference case, because of the lower GFA, the NPC pr. 
GFA is 45 % higher at 23,000 DKK/m² vs. 16,000 DKK/m². 
The GWP optimised variant of case 3 is also close to the sufficiency-based SoS value 
(9 % above) and could probably be reduced below this value by using product specif-
ic EPDs or optimising some of the buildings parts and parameters that have not been 
optimised as part of this thesis (e.g. foundations, systems, windows sizes, etc.). If case 
3 could be optimised to a GWP below the sufficiency-based SoS value, this would mit-
igate some of the disadvantages present with case 4. 
This might also be the case for the GWP optimised variant of case 2. However, as it is 
40 % above the sufficiency-based SoS value, this variant would require a lot more op-
timisation. 
 
It is important to consider the different allocation methods used to achieve the differ-
ent values for SOS, as the allocation methods not only affect the SoS for buildings, but 
also for everything else. If a method allocates a larger share of the SoS to one system, 
then another system gets a smaller share of the SoS. This is for instance the principle 
with the sufficiency-based limits - they look at what systems are needed to fulfil a giv-
en living standard and allocates the SoS to these systems. This in turn means that 
some “unnecessary” systems get a smaller share of the SoS or no share at all. 
 
3.5.2. Net-Zero by 2045 
In 2022 The Danish Government chose to advance the climate target of becoming net-
zero from year 2050 to 2045 and committed to a new goal of a 110 % reduction in 
2050 compared to 1990 (Statsministeriet, 2022). 
 
To assess the possibility of net-zero SFHs in 2045, the variants presented in chapter 
3.5.1 Planetary Boundaries, were reassessed using reduced values for embodied and 
operational GWP to simulate a projection to 2045. These assessments have a refer-
ence period of 50 years from 2045 to 2095. 
For the operational GWP, the 2025 emission factors are used with a starting year of 
2045 and assuming a stagnant emission factor from 2050 to 2095. This shift in as-
sessment period results in a 16 % reduction in the emission factors. 
For the embodied GWP, a 70 % reduction is assumed. This reduction is compared to 
the embodied GWP calculated with existing data today. This reduction assumes that 
all materials are produced using renewable energy. As part of the project “4 TIL 1 
PLANET” by Realdania and Villum Fonden, Artelia A/S made an “inspiration data-
base” assessing different EPDs for different materials. When assessing products with 
two EPDs - one with GOs[2] and one without - Artelia found that the EPDs with GOs 
 
[2] Guarantee of Origin certificate or Green certificate. 
==PAGE:76==
 
Results and Analysis 
 
67 
 
 
 
 
had a 70 % lower GWP on average, compared to the same product without GOs (Ar-
telia A/S & 4 TIL 1 PLANET, 2023). 
As these reductions will not be enough to construct net-zero SFHs, the remaining 
GWP could be abated using Direct Air Carbon Capture and Storage (DAC). The price of 
DAC is between 134 and 342 USD pr. abated ton of CO₂e (Baylin-Stern & Berghout, 
2021). Using the average price and the exchange rate at the time of writing (1 USD = 
6.81 DKK), the price of DAC used for the assessments is 1,620 DKK pr. abated ton of 
CO₂e. The project GWP and DAC costs can be seen in Figure 31. 
NPC will not be assessed in this section; however, the total NPC can be assumed to be 
approximately equal to the NPC shown in Figure 30 plus the DAC costs shown in Fig-
ure 31. 
 
Figure 31 Projected GWP of SFH in 2045 and DAC cost to achieve net-zero GWP. 
See explanation of variant in Table 24. GWP and DAC cost are displayed pr. year. DAC = Direct Air Carbon 
Capture and Storage, DH = District heating, HP = Heat pump, PV = PV-panels. 
 
The projected GWP values shown in Figure 31 ranges from 118 kg CO₂e/year in the 
best variant (C4, HP (100/0)) to 579 kg CO₂e/year in the reference case. These values 
are 44 to 67 % lower than the GWP calculated in 3.5.1 Planetary Boundaries. The 
GWP from operational energy accounts for a significantly larger share of the project-
ed total GWP (17 to 55 percent) compared to the GWP from chapter 3.5.1 (9 to 23 
percent). 
The DAC costs ranges from 191 to 938 DKK/year from best variant to the reference 
case. The total DAC costs over 50 years represents between 0.4 and 1.4 % of the total 
NPC calculated for the variants in chapter 3.5.1. 
Note that the price of DAC is assumed to be the same in 2045 as today, which is likely 
not the case. The DAC cost has also not been discounted to present a Net Present Cost. 
 
These results indicates that it is possible to construct net-zero SFHs in 2045 with rel-
atively minor costs for DAC, when using the assumed projections described above.

3.6. Sensitivity Analysis 
This section assesses the sensitivity of the calculations for the optimisation of insula-
tion thicknesses and the whole building assessment. The sensitivity is assessed by 
varying the GWP and NPC for operational energy use. 
 
Three scenarios are used to assess the sensitivity to change in GWP pr. kWh: 
• 
GWP 1: No changes to GWP/kWh (GWP baseline) 
• 
GWP 2: The reduction in GWP/kWh from 2025 to 2075 are decreased by 50 % 
• 
GWP 3: The GWP/kWh is kept constant at 2025 level 
 
Three other scenarios are used to assess the sensitivity to change in GWP pr. kWh: 
• 
NPC 1: No changes to NPC/kWh (NPC baseline) 
• 
NPC 2: The NPC/kWh is increase by 50 % 
• 
NPC 3: The NPC/kWh is reduced by 50 % 
 
The above scenarios are used to recalculate the optimal insulation thicknesses and 
then used for whole building assessment of two different variants of case building 3. 
The GWP scenarios are applied to a variant of case 3 with insulation thicknesses op-
timised low GWP, while the NPC scenarios are applied to a variant of case 3 with insu-
lation thicknesses optimised low NPC. 
 
GWP 2 and GWP 3 represents a 87 and 174 % increase in GWP/kWh for the reference 
period. However, when optimising the insulation thicknesses for the two scenarios, 
the only result in an increase in optimal insulation thicknesses of 42 and 71 % (medi-
an across all elements), respectively. These increases in optimal thickness are signifi-
cantly, however the increases in thickness are less than half the increases in 
GWP/kWh. This shows that the optimal insulation thicknesses are sensitive to chang-
es to GWP/kWh, but also that the scaling is sublinear. 
 
NPC 2 results in a median increase in optimal insulation thicknesses of 24 %, com-
pared to NPC 1, while NPC 3 results in a decrease of 32 %. These scenarios show the 
same behaviour as the GWP scenarios. The optimal thicknesses are sensitive to 
change in NPC/kWh, but the scaling is sublinear. 
 
To assess the impact of the scenarios on total GWP and NPC, the scenarios are used in 
whole building assessments of case 3. The results from these assessments can be seen 
in Figure 32. 
 
==PAGE:78==
 
Results and Analysis 
 
69 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 32 Sensitivity to changes in GWP and NPC pr. kWh. 
 
For the GWP scenarios, the increase in total GWP is 18 and 33 % for GWP 2 and 3, re-
spectively, and the increase in total NPC is 2 and 3 %. They show a small increase in 
embodied GWP, however the majority of the increase comes from the operational en-
ergy use. There is a small increase in embodied NPC (2 and 4 %) and a slightly larger 
reduction in operation NPC (7 and 12 %). Both are due to increases in insulation 
thicknesses. 
These results shows that a change in GWP/kWh can significantly impact the total 
GWP. The impact comes primary from the increase in GWP from operational energy 
use, and not from the increase in insulation thicknesses. The total NPC only sees a 
minor increase from the change in insulation thicknesses. 
 
For the NPC scenarios, NPC 2 results an increase in total NPC of 4 %, compared to 
baseline, while NPC 3 results in a reduction of 3 %. The embodied NPC are with 1.5 % 
of the baseline for both scenarios, while the change in operational NPC are significant 
at 73 and -52 % for NPC 2 and 3, respectively. The total GWP is within 1 % of the 
baseline. 
These results shows that a change in NPC/kWh does not significantly impact the total 
NPC. The NPC from operational energy use sees a significant increase, but since the 
embodied NPC accounts for more than 90 % of the total NPC, this has a very limited 
impact on the total NPC.

4. Discussion 
The assessments conducted as part of this thesis will be discussed in chapter, examin-
ing opportunities and limitations in the methodological foundation, as well as the rel-
evance of the results and the applicability to the construction industry. Concluding 
this chapter, the actions going forward will be discussed, both in terms of decarbonis-
ing the industry and in terms of the future research needed. 
 
The following themes will be discussed: 
• 
Cost of Conserved Carbon and Levelized Cost of Carbon Abatement 
• 
Attributional and consequential LCA 
• 
The Use of Net Present Cost 
• 
The Role of Energy Frame Calculations in a Future Driven by GWP 
• 
Actions Going Forward 
 
4.1. Cost of Conserved Carbon and Levelized Cost of Car-
bon Abatement 
In this thesis, the potential for Global Warming Potential (GWP) reductions in Single-
Family Houses (SFH) have been assessed with an emphasis on economic efficiency in 
terms of Net Present Value (NPC). Given the urgency of the global climate crisis and 
the uncertain world economy, the exploration of economically efficient alternatives to 
direct GWP reductions in SFHs is crucial. These alternatives include, but are not lim-
ited to, Carbon Capture and Storage (CCS) and investments in renewable energy. 
 
Economically Efficient Alternatives 
Comparing the Levelized Cost of Carbon Abatement (LCCA) of the methods men-
tioned above reveals varied efficiency and feasibility. CCS technologies, while promis-
ing for large-scale emission reductions, are often cited as being too expensive and an 
immature technology (Baylin-Stern & Berghout, 2021). Conversely, investments in 
renewable energy sources, such as wind and solar, have seen a significant decrease in 
cost over the past decade, making them more economically viable (Taylor et al., 
2023). 
 
A list of various LCCA values for different carbon abatement measures is shown in 
Table 4 (chapter 1.4.6, page 13). To compare with the Cost of Conserved Carbon 
(CCC) in SFHs, the average LCCA values from this table will be used. 
To compare the economic efficiency across the technologies in Table 4 and the direct 
GWP reductions in SFHs, the whole building CCC functions shown in Table 17, Figure 
25, and Figure 26 are used to calculate the expected GWP at the same abatement 
costs as the abatement technologies. The results of these calculations can be seen in 
Table 25. 
 
 
==PAGE:80==
 
Discussion

shown in Table 4. 
The values show in parentheses are the GWP/year [kg CO₂e/year]. 
 
Comparing the GWP results shown in Table 25 with the GWP of the reference case, 
the maximum economically efficient direct GWP reduction can be found for each case 
at each CCC. 
For the cheapest LCCA at 475 DKK/ton CO₂e (CCS, iron and steel), it is economically 
efficient to reduce the GWP with 0 to 47 % (depending on case building) compared to 
the reference case. At a LCCA of 1,620 DKK/ton CO₂e (DAC), 11 to 53 % reductions 
are efficient and at 8,950 DKK/ton CO₂e (Shipping and aviation, e-fuel), 30 to 61 % 
reductions are efficient. 
These results suggests that when comparing to the technologies with lower LCCA, 
such as CCS at the CO₂e sources, only a small reduction in GWP is economically effi-
cient. However, if the GFA of the building is reduced compared to the reference case, 
then significantly higher GWP reductions can be economically efficient. 
An aspect to keep in mind, is that while technologies like DAC can technically be in-
stalled in an “infinite” capacity, the capacity for CCS at the CO₂e sources is limited by 
the number of sources and that these CCS plants would probably not be able to abate 
all the carbon emitted by the building sector. 
 
Cumulative GWP over time 
Choosing an alternative GWP reduction method, such as CCS or renewable energy in-
vestment, over direct GWP reductions in SFHs can lead to larger upfront emissions. 
Having large upfronts emissions and using abatement technologies can result in the 
same total cumulative GWP emissions at the end of the reference period, compared to 
a low upfront scenario without abatement. However, the scenario with higher upfront 
emissions would result in a higher concentration of CO₂e being in the atmosphere for 
a longer period of time, which could lead to more damages. To account for this higher 
average concentration, discount rates can be used in the same way as when assessing 
Life Cycle Costs (Sarofim & Giordano, 2018). An example highlighting the effects of 
applying a discount rate to the total cumulative GWP can be seen in Figure 33. 
 
==PAGE:81==
 
Discussion 
 
72 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 33 Effect of discount rate (3.3 %) on total cumulative GWP. 
Comparison of two scenarios with the same total cumulative GWP: 1) High upfront GWP with carbon 
abatement, 2) Low upfront without carbon abatement. In the legend carbon abatement is denominated 
“CCS”. 
 
The example shown in Figure 33 consists of two scenarios with the following prereq-
uisites: 
• 
Scenario 1, High upfront GWP with carbon abatement 
o Upfront GWP: 
100 
o Continuous GWP: 
0.5 pr. year 
o Carbon abatement: 
-1.0 pr. year 
o Total GWP: 
75 after 50 years 
150 after 100 years 
• 
Scenario 2, Low upfront GWP without carbon abatement 
o Upfront GWP: 
50 
o Continuous GWP: 
0.5 pr. year 
o Carbon abatement: 
0.0 pr. year 
o Total GWP: 
75 after 50 years 
150 after 100 years 
To simulate a building with a 50-year reference period, the upfront GWP occurs at 
both year 0 and year 50. Both scenarios are calculated using a discount rate of 0 and 
3.3 % (Sarofim & Giordano, 2018). 
When using a discount rate of 0 percent, the total cumulative GWP are equal in the 
two scenarios after both 50 and 100 years. However, the average GWP over 100 years 
is 25 % higher in scenario 1 compared to scenario 2. 
When using a discount rate of 3.3 percent, the total cumulative GWP after 100 years is 
now significantly lower in both examples, with the total GWP being 99 and 66 (unit-
less GWP) after 100 years for scenario 1 and 2, respectively. In order to close the gap 
in total cumulative GWP, the total abated GWP should be equal to double the differ-
ence in upfront GWP.

While this is just an illustrative example, it clearly shows the importance of consider-
ing GWP over time, when comparing long term strategies for reduction of CO₂e. 
 
Feasibility of Exclusive Use of Carbon Abatement Methods 
Considering the exclusive use of carbon abatement methods, without directly reduc-
ing the GWP of SFHs, poses a challenging proposition. While theoretically possible, 
this approach would likely necessitate an aggressive deployment of CCS technologies 
and renewable energy to offset the operational and embodied carbon of SFHs. Such a 
strategy must be evaluated against the planetary boundary (PB) for GWP, considering 
the cumulative impact and the need for rapid decarbonization across all sectors, as 
most of these boundaries have been transgressed, including the PB for climate change 
(Richardson et al., 2023). 
The feasibility of this approach also hinges on technological advancements, economic 
factors, and policy frameworks that collectively drive the adoption of low-carbon 
technologies. Hanna et al (2021) modelled an “emergency DAC program” with a “war-
time-like crash deployment of DAC”. The emergency DAC program would need in-
vestments of 1.2 to 1.9 % of global GDP annually. With current policy efforts and 
trends (SSP2-4.5), the deployment of the emergency DAC program would limit the 
global warming to 2.4 to 2.5 °C in year 2100, which is well above the 1.5 °C target of 
the Paris Agreement (Hanna et al., 2021). This shows that it is imperative to integrate 
direct GWP reduction measures with broader carbon abatement strategies to ensure 
a holistic and effective approach to staying within global carbon budgets. 
 
In conclusion, the discussion emphasizes the importance of a multifaceted approach 
to reducing the GWP of SFHs in Denmark. While economically efficient alternatives 
such as CCS and renewable energy investments offer viable pathways to reduce glob-
al GWP, they should complement rather than replace direct reduction efforts in the 
building sector. The combined use of direct and indirect reduction strategies will like-
ly be critical in achieving the most economically and environmentally sustainable 
outcomes. 
 
4.2. Attributional and Consequential LCA 
The methodology and results sections of this thesis employed an attributional ap-
proach to LCA for assessing the GWP of (SFH) in Denmark. Attributional LCA (ALCA) 
provides a snapshot of the environmental impacts associated with the production and 
consumption of products or services at a given point in time. This approach has been 
an important tool in identifying key areas within the lifecycle of SFHs where GWP re-
ductions can be achieved effectively. 
 
However, a consequential LCA (CLCA) approach could potentially offer additional in-
sights, particularly in the context of long-term environmental and economic impacts. 
CLCA examines the broader system changes and market effects resulting from deci-
sions, thus providing a dynamic perspective on how GWP reduction measures could 
influence future environmental outcomes (Jolliet et al., 2016). For instance, the adop-
tion of bio-based products could lead to changes in land use and land use change (LU-
LUC), impacting the GWP from biobased products significantly (Hjuler & Hansen, 
2017; Jolliet et al., 2016). 
==PAGE:83==
 
Discussion 
 
74 
 
 
 
 
 
The choice between ALCA and CLCA methodologies can have implications for the as-
sessment of environmental impacts in the building sector. ALCA's focus on direct 
emissions and impacts associated with the construction and use of SFH may overlook 
potential market-driven effects and indirect consequences of material substitutions 
or energy efficiency improvements. In contrast, CLCA could capture the effects of in-
creased demand for low-GWP materials on supply chains and the environment, po-
tentially revealing unintended consequences such as increased pressure on natural 
resources or shifts in land use patterns. 
 
Role of Land Use and Land Use Change in GWP of Biobased Products 
As described in chapter 3.1, most of the materials with the lowest GWP values are 
primarily biobased. The role of Land Use and Land Use Change (LULUC) is particular-
ly relevant when considering the GWP impacts of biobased materials in construction. 
While biobased materials are often touted for their potential to reduce GWP through 
carbon sequestration during growth, the potential changes in land use and how it af-
fects the environmental impacts, should be evaluated. The conversion of forests, agri-
cultural or similar land to produce biobased construction materials can lead to direct 
and/or indirect land use change, which can result in significant increases in GWP due 
to e.g. deforestation, potentially offsetting the GWP benefits of these materials (Hjuler 
& Hansen, 2017). 
 
The differences in GWP results in ALCA and CLCA is evident when comparing BUILD 
Report 2023:12 and BUILD Report 2023:22. Report 2023:12 showcases best practice 
cases for GWP calculated using ALCA and most of the SFH cases have a larger than 
normal share of biobased materials (Garnow et al., 2023). Report 2023:22 uses CLCA 
to compare the environmental impacts of shifting from conventional to timber-based 
construction in Denmark (Hansen, Andersen, et al., 2023). While Report 2023:12 
(ALCA-based) highlights these primarily biobased SFHs as best practice cases, Report 
2023:22 (CLCA-based) finds the opposite to be true with an increase of around 50 % 
when shifting to timber-based construction. 
 
Applications of ALCA and CLCA in the Construction Industry 
The choice between ALCA and CLCA depends on the specific objectives of the assess-
ment, the scope of decision-making, and the intended application of the results. 
 
ALCA is well suited for consultants, whose primary tasks is to evaluate the GWP of a 
diverse portfolio of buildings, as it is a simpler and faster approach to LCA, which in 
the end results in a lower cost for the customer. ALCA are particularly useful for: 
• 
Identifying hotspots for GWP reduction in specific building projects 
• 
Comparing the environmental performance of different construction materials 
 
and technologies 
• 
Faster assessment resulting in lower cost 
For consultants evaluating GWP across numerous projects, ALCA offers a standard-
ized methodology for assessing opportunities to lower emissions on a project-by-
project basis and providing case specific recommendations for material selection, de-
sign changes, and energy efficiency measures. 
 
==PAGE:84==
 
Discussion 
 
75 
 
 
 
 
CLCA is more suited for assessing the broader implications of strategic decisions 
within the construction industry. By considering the potential market and system-
wide effects of adopting new materials, technologies, or practices, CLCA provides in-
sights into: 
• 
The likely environmental consequences of shifts in material demand, including 
 
LULUC, resource availability, and market dynamics 
• 
The effectiveness of industry-wide adoption of low-GWP materials or 
 
energy-efficient technologies in achieving broader environmental objectives 
• 
Opportunities for systemic changes within the construction industry that align 
 
with relevant sustainability goals 
The forward-looking perspective of CLCA makes it particularly useful for industry 
stakeholders who aims to understand the broader environmental implications of var-
ious products and systems. Policymakers and researchers can use CLCA to identify 
which areas, materials, and technologies has the highest potential for reducing the 
industry's overall GWP. By highlighting the potential unintended consequences of 
certain strategies (such as increased demand leading to resource depletion or land 
use changes), CLCA informs more sustainable decision-making at the industry level.  
Due to the dynamic and forward-looking perspective, CLCA is significantly more 
complicated and time-consuming than ALCA, which makes it impractical for some 
applications and significantly more expensive to perform. 
 
The above discussion about the implications of the ALCA and CLCA methodologies 
highlights the complexity of assessing and optimizing the environmental impacts of 
SFH in Denmark. While ALCA has provided valuable insights into direct GWP reduc-
tion opportunities, a CLCA perspective is necessary to understand the broader envi-
ronmental implications of these strategies, especially concerning the use of biobased 
materials and the importance of LULUC. Future research should aim to integrate both 
ALCA and CLCA approaches to develop a comprehensive understanding of the most 
sustainable practices for reducing the GWP of SFH, taking into account direct and in-
direct environmental impacts. 
 
4.3. The Use of Net Present Cost 
The use of Net Present Cost (NPC) as an economic assessment tool in this thesis pro-
vides a nuanced perspective beyond the initial procurement costs. This thesis sys-
tematically explores the implications of NPC in the context of reducing. This approach 
of integrating Life Cycle Costs (LCC) calculations alongside LCA, underscores the sig-
nificance of a holistic approach to environmental and financial sustainability. 
 
Advantages and Disadvantages of Using NPC 
The primary advantage of utilizing NPC is its ability to evaluate over the entire lifecy-
cle of a building, including initial construction, operation, maintenance, and end-of-
life phases. The inclusion of these lifecycle phases is important to assess the actual 
economic cost of building solutions. 
NPC also enables a more accurate reflection of the economic value of energy conser-
vation measures and low-carbon solutions. By discounting future costs and benefits 
to their present value, NPC facilitates a balanced consideration of upfront invest-
ments against long-term savings, which is crucial when assessing sustainable building 
==PAGE:85==
 
Discussion 
 
76 
 
 
 
 
practices. This is in line with the research objectives aimed at identifying economical-
ly feasible strategies for reducing GWP in SFHs without compromising on the NPC. 
Despite the above advantages, the application of NPC is not without limitations. The 
reliance on discount rates and future cost projections introduces a level of uncertain-
ty, especially over the long reference period of 50 years used in this study. The choice 
of a 3 % discount rate, while common, may not fully reflect the volatility and risk as-
sociated with future economic conditions or the continuous improvement of technol-
ogy. The focus on financial metrics could also inadvertently overshadow other quali-
tative benefits of sustainable building practices, such as potential improved indoor 
environmental quality, improved health, and resilience against climate change. 
 
The Choice of Discount Rate 
A discount rate of 3 % was used for all NPC calculations. The selection of a discount 
rate always warrants a critical examination. Discount rates are important in LCC as-
sessments as they significantly influence the NPC, acting as a proxy for the value of 
money over time and the risk associated with future investments. 
Using a 3 % discount rate represents a middle-ground on the future value and associ-
ated risks. It strikes a balance between being too optimistic and too conservative, 
making it a pragmatic choice for evaluating sustainable building practices. This allows 
for an assessment of long-term sustainability measures while still acknowledging the 
value of future saving. A discount rate of 3 % is also reasonably close the recom-
mended discount rate of 3.5 % for reference periods up to 50 years by Boardman et 
al. (E. Boardman et al., 2018; Moore et al., 2004). 
However, the adoption of a 3 % rate is not devoid of critique. One could argue that the 
discount rate should be higher to represent the opportunity cost of capital (e.g. stock 
market) or to represent a high inflation environment and the associated uncertainty 
and risk premiums. Others could argue that a lower discount rate, should be used to 
represent a low-interest-rate environment where the reduced cost of borrowing 
could incentivize upfront investments in sustainability or energy conserving 
measures. 
 
The above discussion about the use of NPC and the selection of a 3 % discount rate 
highlights the complexity of financial decision-making in sustainable building practic-
es. While NPC offers a valuable framework for assessing the economic viability of 
low-GWP SFHs, it is important to recognize the inherent uncertainties and biases as-
sociated with discount rates and financial projections. Further research could explore 
the impact of varying discount rates on NPC outcomes and investigate alternative fi-
nancial models. 
 
4.4. The Role of Energy Frame Calculations in a Future 
Driven by GWP 
An aspect of this investigation has been the evaluation of energy frame calculations 
within the broader context of LCA regulations and the move towards renewable en-
ergy systems. The findings suggest a need to reassess the role of energy frame calcu-
lations, especially considering the evolving regulatory landscape and the urgent need 
to mitigate climate change. 
==PAGE:86==
 
Discussion 
 
77 
 
 
 
 
 
This thesis explores the balance between GWP and NPC of SFHs and how that balance 
of economic and environmental performance is affected by energy efficiency 
measures and different forms of energy supplies. One aspect of the investigations has 
been the assessment of optimal insulation thicknesses and the impacts from heat 
supply and renewable energy systems. The findings suggest that some aspects of the 
energy frame somewhat hinder the ability to fully optimise for low GWP. 
 
A Potential Misalignment 
Energy frame calculations, as mandated by the Danish Building Regulations (BR18), 
have historically played a crucial role in achieving better energy efficiency of build-
ings. However, the primary focus on energy efficiency, without directly considering 
GWP, appears partly misaligned with a sustainability objective of achieving lower 
GWP. The analysis presented in this thesis, particularly the assessment of optimal in-
sulation thickness, underlines the potential limitations of current energy frame calcu-
lations, as the results show that the optimal insulations thicknesses (for both GWP 
and NPC) are generally lower than what is presently considered the norm. 
The assessment of PV-panels presents a similar misalignment between the energy 
frame and the possibility for reducing GWP and NPC. When using lower (optimal) in-
sulation thicknesses, the energy frame often dictates the use of PV-panels or heat 
pumps (or similar), which increases GWP and/or NPC. The energy frame also makes 
it almost practically impossible to use direct electric heating at reasonable U-values. 
While direct electric heating does generally result in higher GWP, it does result in a 
lower total NPC compared to heat pumps and the lowest overall embodied NPC. 
There is also plenty of ways to design SFH with direct electric heating that achieves 
lower GWP and NPC than the reference case. 
 
The introduction of LCA regulations in BR18 represents a significant step forward in 
assessing the climate crisis and evaluating the environmental performance of build-
ings in terms of GWP. While energy frame calculations focus on operational energy 
use, the LCA regulations focus on both the operational and embodied GWP. This more 
holistic approach is crucial for identifying and implementing measures that genuinely 
reduce the environmental footprint of SFHs. Nonetheless, energy frame calculations 
remain valuable for optimizing operational energy efficiency, which is still an im-
portant step, as more efficient building would result in a faster transition to renewa-
ble energy, since the energy demand would be lower. Therefore, integrating energy 
frame calculations with LCA regulations can provide a more comprehensive frame-
work for sustainable building design and operation. 
 
Integration of Energy Frame and LCA 
Allowing for flexibility in exceeding the energy frame (if a lower total GWP can be 
documented) could incentivize innovative approaches to building design and con-
struction. This approach aligns with the principle of cost-optimal levels of energy effi-
ciency where environmental and economic considerations are balanced to achieve 
sustainable outcomes. The findings of this thesis demonstrate the potential for energy 
related optimisations to significantly influence both GWP and NPC, suggesting that a 
more flexible regulatory framework could facilitate research and adoption of solu-
tions that are more tailored to the specific sustainability goals of project individually. 
==PAGE:87==
 
Discussion 
 
78 
 
 
 
 
The shift towards renewable energy systems is integral to reducing the GWP of build-
ings. The findings from this thesis highlight the potential for renewable and efficient 
energy systems, such as heat pumps, to lower the operational GWP of SFHs. However, 
in some cases the use of renewable energy systems results in an increase in total 
GWP, such is the case with PV-panels. Implementing a requirement for renewable en-
ergy systems used in energy frame calculations to demonstrate an overall negative 
GWP when considering the case specific life cycle, could accelerate the transition to 
low-carbon buildings. Such a requirement could encourage the adoption of technolo-
gies that not only meet energy efficiency standards but also contribute to a reduction 
in the overall GWP of buildings. 
 
In conclusion, this thesis underscores the need for a critical reassessment of energy 
frame calculations within the evolving context of building regulations and sustainabil-
ity goals. While these calculations remain a valuable tool for promoting energy effi-
ciency, integrating them with broader LCA requirements and adopting a more flexi-
ble, holistic approach to building sustainability could enhance their relevance and ef-
fectiveness in reducing the GWP of SFHs in Denmark. Future regulations should con-
sider the interdependencies between energy efficiency, renewable energy systems, 
and overall environmental impact, fostering a regulatory environment that encour-
ages innovation, flexibility, and sustainability in the building sector. 
 
4.5. Actions Going Forward 
The results of this thesis highlight the importance of adopting low-GWP materials and 
practices, alongside an emphasis on reducing the GFA and surface-to-GFA ratio of 
SFHs to achieve dual benefits: minimization of environmental impact and optimiza-
tion of economic costs. 
 
Lowering GWP and NPC through Material Innovation and Government Inter-
ventions 
The findings suggest that a significant reduction in GWP can be achieved by transi-
tioning towards low-GWP materials. However, a negative exponential correlation be-
tween GWP and NPC, resulting the mor significant reductions becoming exponentially 
more expensive. Economies of scale and government subsidies could play a crucial 
role in making these materials more affordable, thereby encouraging their wide-
spread adoption. Innovations in production methods, such as the utilization of re-
newable energy sources and carbon capture technologies, offer a pathway to reduce 
the GWP of traditionally high-impact materials. This dual approach (lowering the cost 
of low-GWP materials while simultaneously reducing the GWP of low-NPC materials) 
presents a viable strategy for aligning economic and environmental goals. 
 
Addressing the Trend of Increasing GFA 
The trend towards larger SFHs poses a significant challenge in reducing both GWP 
and NPC. This thesis indicates that reversing this trend by promoting smaller, more 
efficient homes could lead to substantial reductions in both areas. As discussed in Pe-
tersen & Hatic (2023), using a limit value expressed as kg CO₂e/m²/year is socially 
unfair, as building larger SFH makes it easier to comply with the regulations and re-
sults in higher absolute GWP (Petersen & Hatic, 2023). Adjusting the regulatory 
==PAGE:88==
 
Discussion 
 
79 
 
 
 
 
framework to introduce a new limit value that accounts for this phenomenon could 
mitigate the advantages larger buildings currently have. This could be done by using a 
factor based on GFA as proposed by Petersen & Hatic (2023), by adding a supplement 
like with the energy frame, or by simple using an absolute limit value. Examples of 
each approach can be seen below: 
• 
GFA factor:
