The three examples are constructed to achieve a limit value of 7.5 kg CO₂e/m²/year 
for a GFA of 160 m². With the three examples a GFA of 213 m² would get a limit value 
of 5.6, 6.4 and 5.6 kg CO₂e/m²/year, respectively. The values used are only for illus-
trational purposes. 
This approach would encourage the design and construction of buildings that are 
both environmentally sustainable and economically viable and discourage the con-
struction of bigger buildings. 
 
Future Research 
Future research could focus on expanding the methodological framework to include 
Consequential Life Cycle Assessment (CLCA) for a comprehensive understanding of 
the broader environmental impacts from the decisions made in the optimisation pro-
cesses, and the scope should be expanded to include other building typologies. To 
prevent a hyperfocus on GWP, more impact categories should be included to enable a 
more holistic assessment of the sustainability, which is paramount as six out of nine 
PB have been transgressed (Richardson et al., 2023). 
Future research could also benefit from more thoroughly investigating the sensitivity 
of the assessments, as the sensitivity analysis in chapter 3.6 found that the results 
were sensitive to change in especially GWP pr. kWh. 
Investigating the longevity of buildings and comparing the impacts of new low-GWP 
constructions versus low-GWP refurbishments are also essential in developing a sus-
tainable building strategy that considers both environmental and economic factors. 
 
In conclusion, the path to reducing the GWP of SFHs, while managing NPC, involves a 
multifaceted approach that encompasses material innovation, regulatory adjust-
ments, and expanded research. The integration of environmental and economic con-
siderations in the design and construction of SFHs is crucial for achieving sustainabil-
ity goals. This thesis lays the groundwork for further exploration and implementation 
of strategies that balance the reduction of environmental impact with economic fea-
sibility. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:89==
 
Practical Guideline for optimising GWP and NPC 
 
80 
 
 
 
 
5. Practical Guideline for optimising GWP and 
NPC 
This chapter presents a practical guideline for the Danish construction industry to op-
timise Single-Family Houses (SFH) for low Global Warming Potential (GWP) and Net 
Present Costs (NPC) though strategic design and material choices, based on the find-
ings from this thesis. 
 
Step 1: Preliminary Considerations 
• 
Evaluate the Need for New Construction: Assess whether constructing a new 
 
building is essential. Consider alternatives such as low GWP optimised 
 
refurbishment of existing buildings or opting for e.g. terraced housing[3] to meet 
 
the same needs with potentially lower environmental and economic costs. 
• 
Gross Floor Area (GFA) Optimisation: Strive to reduce the GFA as much as 
 
practically possible. Smaller GFA directly contribute to significant reductions in 
 
both GWP and NPC by limiting the amounts of materials and energy consumed. 
 
Step 2: Design Optimisation 
• 
Building Geometry: Design buildings with a low surface-to-GFA ratio to reduce 
 
material intensity and operational energy use, thereby decreasing both GWP 
 
and NPC. 
• 
Variant Study on Construction Materials: 
o Conduct a comprehensive assessment of construction materials 
considering both their environmental impact and total lifecycle cost. 
o Expand the material selection beyond conventional choices, exploring 
innovative and low-impact options. 
o Utilise the Cost of Conserved Carbon (CCC) concept as a decision-making 
tool to evaluate the economic viability of materials, setting clear NPC 
limits or prioritising materials with a favourable CCC. 
 
Step 3: Energy Consideration and Conservation Measures 
• 
Energy Supply Type: 
o Evaluate the GWP and NPC implications of different energy supply 
systems. Heat pumps are preferable for low GWP but may have a higher 
NPC, while district heating can offer a balanced approach. Direct electric 
heating, generally leading to high GWP, should be used cautiously. 
o Incorporate PV-panels and other renewable energy systems selectively, 
aiming for an overall reduction in GWP. 
• 
Energy Conserving Measures: While not directly covered in this thesis, 
 
incorporating additional energy-saving strategies is crucial. Consider air 
 
tightness, reducing thermal bridges, optimising SFP-values, improving window 
 
efficiency, strategic window placement, and adopting low-temperature heating 
 
systems. 
 
 
 
[3] Terraced housing: På dansk ‘rækkehuse’. 
==PAGE:90==
 
Practical Guideline for optimising GWP and NPC 
 
81 
 
 
 
 
Step 4: Insulation Optimisation 
• 
Optimise Insulation Thickness for GWP and NPC: Carefully determine the 
 
optimal insulation thickness by considering its impact on both GWP and NPC. 
 
This can be approached by evaluating the effects of minor adjustments in 
 
insulation thickness or by conducting a more detailed assessment using heat 
 
degree day (HDD) or simulation software to explore a range of thicknesses. 
 
Conclusion and Final Considerations 
This guide aims to equip the Danish construction industry with a holistic approach to 
designing Single-Family Houses (SFH) that are both economically and environmental-
ly sustainable. By prioritising low GWP and NPC through strategic design and materi-
al choices, we can make significant strides towards mitigating climate change. 
These guidelines should be applied throughout the design and construction process 
to ensure that all decisions align with the overarching objectives. It is important to 
monitor the performance of buildings post-construction, both in terms of energy per-
formance to limit unnecessary energy use, but also in terms of the general environ-
mental and economic performance to refine future projects and contribute to a 
broader knowledge base on sustainable construction practices. 
While this guideline serves as a general framework for optimising GWP and NPC in 
SFH design, it is crucial to understand that every building project is unique. There-
fore, all recommended measures and strategies should be evaluated and adjusted 
based on the specific context, requirements, and constraints of each individual pro-
ject. 
Consider broadening the scope to incorporate a wider spectrum of environmental 
(and social) impact categories into the optimisation process. These may include bio-
diversity loss, acidification, eutrophication, the release of novel entities (such as mi-
croplastics or nanomaterials), and more. Each of these impact categories represents 
different dimensions of environmental degradation and has its own set of challenges 
and solutions. By considering these additional categories, we can ensure a more holis-
tic approach to sustainability that not only mitigates climate change but also pre-
serves the planet's ecosystems and biodiversity. 
It is also crucial to recognise that the journey towards sustainability does not end 
with the construction of a building. Sustainable living extends into every aspect of our 
daily lives, from the choices we make as consumers to the ways we engage with our 
communities and advocate for systemic change. The construction of a building might 
be a one-time event, but its impact, both environmentally and socially, lasts for gen-
erations. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:91==
 
Practical Guideline for optimising GWP and NPC 
 
82 
 
 
 
 
Further Considerations Beyond Construction 
While optimising the GWP from the construction industry is crucial, sustainability ex-
tends beyond the boundaries of the buildings themselves, as buildings are only one 
part of the current challenges the World is facing. To fully adopt a sustainable way of 
living, take into account these wider considerations: 
• 
Sustainable Living: Be mindful of the impacts from your household and your 
 
daily consumption habits. Seek out information about sustainable living from 
 
reputable sources. 
• 
Engagement with Community: Advocate for and utilise sustainable modes of 
 
transportation. Support and engage in community or urban planning efforts that 
 
promote walkability, cycling, and public transport. Participate in or initiate 
 
community-based energy projects, such as collective solar power installations 
 
or local energy cooperatives. 
• 
Promote Sustainability: Share knowledge and experiences related to 
 
sustainability with your network and encourage to adoption of more 
 
sustainable habits. Use potential professional influence to advocate for 
 
sustainable practices within your field and encourage colleagues and industry 
 
partners to consider the environmental implications of their work. 
• 
Ecological Conservation and Restoration: Engage with and support ecological 
 
restoration projects and conservation efforts. These actions contribute to 
 
carbon sequestration, biodiversity, and natural ecosystems. Where possible, 
 
create or participate in the development of green spaces within your 
 
community. Urban greenery, such as parks, green roofs, and gardens, can help 
 
mitigate the urban heat island effect, improve air quality, and potentially 
 
support local biodiversity. 
• 
Demand Actions: Use your vote to support leaders and policies that prioritise 
 
climate action and sustainable development goals. Actively demand stronger 
 
environmental policies and regulations from local, national, and international 
 
politicians and policymakers. Support legislation that aims to reduce 
 
environmental impacts. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:92==
 
Conclusion 
 
83 
 
 
 
 
6. Conclusion 
In this thesis, the possibilities for reducing the Global Warming Potential (GWP) of 
Single-Family Houses (SFH) in Denmark have been thoroughly investigated, focusing 
on the dual objectives of minimizing Global Warming Potential (GWP) while also con-
sidering the economic implications, as measured by the Net Present Cost (NPC). 
The main objective of this thesis was to “investigate the feasibility of designing a low-
GWP SFH that maintains an equal or lower NPC compared to a reference case reflect-
ing conventional practices”. 
 
Research Findings and Summary 
The findings of this thesis suggests that substantial reductions in GWP are indeed fea-
sible through various means, including optimization of insulation thicknesses and se-
lection of building materials with lower embodied carbon, while the most effective 
measures were reductions in Gross Floor Area (GFA) and surface-to-GFA ratio. These 
strategies not only contribute to a lower GWP but also to an overall reduction in NPC 
in most cases, highlighting the economic viability of sustainable building practices. 
A critical aspect of this research was the development of Cost of Conserved Carbon 
(CCC) functions, clearly showcasing the negative exponential correlation between 
GWP and NPC when considering different materials. The relationship between GWP 
and NPC do however become increasingly complex when compared the results across 
different building cases. These CCC functions paved the way for a discussion concern-
ing the viability of direct GWP reductions in the construction sector compared to in-
direct carbon abatement technologies. The potential for building SFHs within the PB 
for GWP was investigated and data indicates, depending on allocation method, it is 
possible, but requires a 50 %reduction in GWP. The potential for building net-zero 
SFHs in 2045 was also investigated and found that it was possible with a relatively 
minor cost increase from the use of carbon abatement technologies. 
 
Reflection on the Research Process 
The research process involved a comprehensive review of existing literature, devel-
opment of a detailed methodology for optimising different aspects of SFHs for lower 
GWP and NPC, and application of these methodologies to a set of case buildings, in-
cluding a reference case representative of a typical Danish SFH. This process allowed 
for a nuanced understanding of the interplay between GWP reduction and its implica-
tions on NPC, revealing the complexities and challenges inherent in optimizing build-
ing practices for sustainability.  
 
Contributions to the Field 
This thesis contributes with new knowledge to the field of architectural engineering 
by providing a detailed analysis of the economic implications of reducing GWP in 
SFHs in Denmark. The adaption of the CCE concept to a method for calculating the 
CCC in a building context represents a significant contribution, offering a novel tool 
for assessing the cost-effectiveness of GWP reduction measures. Furthermore, this re-
search provides actionable insights for policymakers, architects, and builders aiming 
to transition towards more sustainable building practices. This thesis also includes a 
practically orienteered Guideline for optimising SFHs for low GWP and NPC, which 
provides architects, engineers, and policymakers with tangible steps for reducing 
GWP and NPC in the Danish construction industry. 
==PAGE:93==
 
Conclusion 
 
84 
 
 
 
 
Recommendations for Future Work 
Future research should focus on employing Consequential Life Cycle Assessment for a 
broader environmental impact understanding and extending the scope to other build-
ing typologies and as well as building refurbishments. Additional impact categories 
beyond GWP should be considered for a holistic sustainability assessment, offering a 
more comprehensive understanding of the sustainability of buildings and mitigating a 
hyperfocus on GWP. 
Material manufacturers should focus on the improving the limiting parameter of their 
products to mitigate the negative correlation of GWP and NPC and achieve construc-
tion products that have both low GWP and NPC, potentially with help from govern-
ment intervention. A cultural shift is needed to address the ongoing trend of building 
increasingly larger SFHs. This change can be helped by designing smaller but better 
buildings that people want to live in and by revising the regulatory framework to 
make it advantageous to construct more sustainable buildings. 
 
Hypotheses Revisited 
1. Negative Correlation between GWP and NPC: The analysis provided nuanced in-
sights into the relationship between GWP and NPC. As hypothesised, a negative corre-
lation was indeed found, but it proved to be a complex topic, as some scenarios could 
achieve reductions in GWP alongside NPC. This was particularly evident in optimising 
the building geometry and insulation thicknesses. 
2. Optimal Solutions for Low GWP and NPC: The research confirmed the existence of 
"sweet spot" solutions that achieve relatively low GWP without incurring dispropor-
tionately high NPC. Through strategic material selection and design optimization, this 
thesis identified feasible pathways to constructing SFHs that meet and exceed current 
sustainability benchmarks without substantial cost premiums. These findings vali-
date the hypothesis and offer practical insights for achieving optimal environmental 
and economic outcomes. 
3. Optimal Insulation Thickness: The investigation into optimal insulation thickness 
revealed a delicate balance between reducing heat loss (and thus operational GWP) 
and the embodied GWP of insulation materials. Through case specific calculations, we 
identified specific insulation thicknesses that minimize total GWP and NPC, affirming 
the hypothesis and providing clear guidelines for designers and builders. 
4. Ability to Build within the Planetary Boundary: The findings of this thesis suggests 
that it is possible to build within the Planetary Boundary for GWP. However, it is 
greatly dependent on allocation method and requires compromises in terms of NPC, 
GFA, and material choices. These finding are pivotal, offering an optimistic but cau-
tious perspective on the construction industry's potential to contribute positively to 
climate change mitigation. 
 
In conclusion, this thesis demonstrates that it is both feasible and economically viable 
to significantly reduce the GWP of SFH in Denmark. By adopting a holistic approach 
that considers environmental as well as economic factors, this research provides a 
roadmap for achieving more sustainable building practices that contribute to the 
broader goal of mitigating climate change. As Denmark and the World at large strive 
towards sustainability, the findings of this thesis offer a valuable guide for navigating 
the complexities of building sustainably in the 21st century.

Appendix 
 
94 
 
 
 
 
 
Appendix 
This chapter contains the appendices for this thesis. The appendices include text, as-
sumptions, data, results, graphs, or other types of supplementary material that is con-
sidered relevant for this thesis, but not considered essential for the main text. These 
additional materials provide deeper insight into prerequisites and findings of this 
thesis, ensuring that the reader has access to all the necessary background or sup-
portive data. 
 
If any data seems relevant but is not part of the appendices, feel free to contact the 
author for more information. 
 
 
==PAGE:104==
 
Appendix 
 
95 
 
 
 
 
A 
Assumptions 
In this appendix, all assumptions that have the potential to impact the findings of the 
thesis, or seems otherwise relevant, can be found. This appendix is divided into sec-
tions for each relevant theme of the thesis. 
 
A.1 
Case Buildings 
• 
Windows: 
o When sizing the windows, the ratio of window area to floor area is used to 
calculate the necessary window area. 
o To simplify the process, the same overall correction factor of 0.65 
(including glass to frame ratio) have been used for all windows in all 
cases. This value has  been chosen as a representative correction factor 
based on overall evaluation of the case buildings. 
• 
Room height: 
o A internal room height of 2400 mm was used for all case buildings. 
 
A.2 
LCA and LCC 
• 
Insulation 
o λ-value for straw insulation is based on EcoCocon. 
o λ-values for other insulation materials are based on values specified in 
environmental data if available, or else on data from known 
manufactures. 
o Wages for insulation with fibrous batts is assumed to be the same as with 
mineral wool batts. 
o Wages for insulation in grounddeck is the average of insulation in wood 
framing and insulation in floors. 
o Wages for insulation with biogenic losefill is assumed to be the same as 
with paper wool losefill. 
o Wages for plastic insulation in batts/sheets is assumed to be the same as 
with EPS batts/sheets. 
o Delivery for straw insulation is assumed to be 1 kr./kg. 
o Wages for construction of wall using hempcrete blocks is assumed to be 
the same as construction of wall using aerated concrete blocks. 
o Wages for external insulation with rigid batts is assumed to be the same 
as with rigid mineral wool batts. 
• 
Surfaces 
o Wages for lime paint are equal to wages for acrylic paint. 
o Wages for 1 layer of clay board are equal to wages for 1 layer of gypsum 
fibre board. 
o Wages for clay plaster are equal to wages for "conventional" plaster. 
o Wages for woodfibre installation layer are equal to wages for 1 layer of 
gypsum fibre board. 
o Wages for PS mounting plate and strips (for low height floor heating 
systems) are assumed to be the same as wages for 30 mm tracked EPS 
boards. 
==PAGE:105==
 
Appendix 
 
96 
 
 
 
 
o Anhydrite and cement-based floor screeds are assumed to have the same 
wages for m2 as "Afretningslag, gulve > 8 m² (15 mm, Weber Floor 319 
inkl. primer)" and the same material price pr. m3. 
o All paints used are lime paints, unless surface includes clay plaster, then 
paint is clay paint. 
o Environmental data for PS mounting plate and strips (for low height floor 
heating systems) are based on XPS insulation. 
• 
Structural systems 
o Traditional wood framing (external wall) contains 12% wood. 
o Double stud wood framing (external wall) contains 6% wood. 
o I-beam wood framing (external wall) contains 6% wood. 
o Traditional wood framing used as non-structural part (external wall) 
contains 10% wood. 
o Traditional wood framing (internal wall) contains 10% wood. 
o Traditional wood framing (ground deck) contains 10% wood. 
o I-beam wood framing (ground deck) contains 5% wood. 
o Steel framing (external wall) have a λ-value = 0.048 when used with 
insulation with a λ-value = 0.037. 
o Steel framing (internal wall) have a weight of 0.86 kg/m and a length of 
2.22 m/m². 
o All roof and floor systems are dimensioned according to the relevant 
manufacturers structural estimate tables or according to structural 
estimate tables from TRÆInformation. 
o Wages for CLT prefab. wall elements are assumed to be the same as wages 
for "normal" wood prefab. wall elements. 
o Wages for Porotherm blocks are assumed to be the same as wages for 
aerated concrete blocks. 
o Wages for hempcrete blocks are assumed to be the same as wages for 
aerated concrete blocks. 
o Wages for CLT and rib prefab. floor elements are assumed to be the same 
as wages for "normal" wood prefab. floor elements. 
 
A.3 
Operational Energy Use 
• 
Energy frame calculations: 
o All prerequisites used for the energy frame calculations can be seen in 
appendix D. 
 
A.4 
Optimisation of Insulation Thicknesses 
• 
When calculating the price for different insulation thicknesses, the wages for 
 
installation of insulation are kept constant (at the wages for U = 0.10), while the 
 
material price is varied according to thickness.

D 
Prerequisites for Energy Frame Calculations 
This appendix showcases the prerequisites and assumptions used when performing 
the energy frame calculations used to estimate the operational energy use for the case 
buildings. 
 
Areas 
Areas are measured in the Revit models made for the case buildings and according to 
DS 418. 
The rotation of the buildings is set to 0 (rotations is the same as stated in chapter 2.1 
Case Buildings). 
 
Heat capacity 
The heat capacity of the case buildings is kept constant in all calculations and is set to 
47 Wh/(m²·K). 
 
Windows and doors 
The following values are used for windows and doors in the calculations: 
Type 
[-]

[-]

[W/(m²·K)]

[-]

[-] 
Solar shading coefficient 
[-]

0.80 
0.65 
0.80 
0.80* 
Window 
S, E, W 
0.80 
0.55 
0.80 
0.80* 
Door 
N 
1.00 
0.65 
0.80 
0.80* 
Door 
S, E, W 
1.00 
0.55 
0.80 
0.80* 
Table 28 Default values used for windows and doors in the energy frame calculations. 
*Corresponding to internal solar shading (e.g. curtains). 
 
The values for shadows are the following: 
• 
Horizon: 
 
20 ° 
(area with same height buildings)  
• 
Eaves: 
 
30 ° 
(normal overhang) 
• 
Left: 
 
0 ° 
(no shadow) 
• 
Right: 
 
0 ° 
(no shadow) 
• 
Window opening: 
10 ° 
(normal window opening) 
 
PV-panels 
When PV-panels are used in the calculations, the following values are used: 
• 
Area: 
 
1.95 m² pr. panel 
• 
Peak power:  
0.2074 kW/m² (corresponding to 400 W pr. panel) 
• 
System efficiency: 
90 % 
• 
Orientation:  
South 
• 
Slope: 
 
15 ° 
• 
Horizon cutoff: 
10 ° 
• 
Left shadow:  
0 ° 
• 
Right shadow: 
0 ° 
 
The number of panels are adjusted to comply with the relevant limit value for the en-
ergy frame calculation. 
 
U-values and thermal bridges 
==PAGE:112==
 
Appendix 
 
103 
 
 
 
 
U-values are adjusted according to the elements being assed. 
 
For thermal bridges, the following values are used (all units are W/(m·K)): 
• 
Foundation under wall: 
0.13 
(expanded clay blocks with insulation) 
• 
Foundation under window: 0.19 
(expanded clay blocks with insulation) 
• 
Foundation under door: 
0.19 
(expanded clay blocks with insulation) 
• 
Window-wall connection: 0.01 
(well insulation connection*) 
• 
Door-wall connection: 
0.01 
(well insulation connection*) 
 
* The window-wall and door-wall connections are dependent on the wall type, how-
ever, must modern wall types can achieve a well insulation connection with a thermal 
bridge loss of 0.01 W/(m·K). 
 
Airtightness 
The values used for airtightness corresponding to a very airtight building to a blower 
door test score of 0.5 l/(s·m²) @ 50 Pa. 
 
Internal heat gains 
The standard values for internal heat gains according to SBi 213 are used: 
• 
People: 
 
1.5 W/m² 
• 
Equipment and lightning: 3.5 W/m² 
 
Ventilation 
Ventilation is assumed to be done with a balanced mechanical ventilation system with 
heat recovery. The following values are used: 
Parameter 
Unit 
Ventilation rate 
Base ventilation rate 
High ventilation rate 
Ventilation rate 
[l/(s·m²)] 
0.30 
0.47-0.93* 
Operating time 
[%]

[%] 
80**

SFP 
[J/m³] 
800 
1200 
Table 29 Default values used for mechanical ventilation in the energy frame calculations. 
*Ventilation rate is case dependent. **Heat recovery displayed is after adjusting for fan heat and de-icing 
according to SBi 213. 
The is assumed that an electric heat coil is assumes as a preheater to mitigate icing of 
the heat recovery unit. 
Besides mechanical ventilation, it is assumed that it is possible to ventilate the build-
ings via natural ventilation with a ventilation rate of to 1.8 l/(s·m²), when necessary. 
 
A thermal loss from insulated ventilation ducts with cold air (exhaust and intake 
duct) inside the heated building envelope is included in the calculation. The values 
used corresponds to 4 m of duct with a thermal loss of 0.5 W/(m·K). 
 
Heating system 
Three different types of heating systems are used in the calculations: District heating 
(DH), heat pump (HP), and direct electric heating (EH). While the EH system does not 
have a distribution system, the distribution system for the DH and HP system are al-
most identical with the following values: 
• 
Supply temp.: 
35 °C 
• 
Return temp.: 
30 °C

• 
Pipe length:  
2 m 
•

0.20 W/(m∙K) 
The DH system also have a heat loss of 0.50 W/K from a DH unit and a time-
controlled circulation pump for the radiant floor heating with an effect of 25 W and a 
reduction factor of 0.30. For the HP system the circulation pump is included in the HP 
values, which are the following:

F 
Optimisation of insulation thicknesses 
 
F.1 
Optimal building elements and their insulation thicknesses, GWP, 
and NPC 
The table below shows the insulation thicknesses, U-values, GWP, and NPC of the op-
timal building element variants after optimising the insulation thicknesses. The vari-
ant name describes the element variant, optimisation scenario, case building, and 
heat supply used in the optimisation process and should be read as follows: 
• 
X##_YYY_C#_ZZ 
o X## 
Element variant (see appendix E.2 for build-up) 
▪ 
E = External wall 
▪ 
G = Ground deck 
▪ 
R = Roof 
o YYY 
Optimisation scenario 
▪ 
GWP = Insulation thickness optimised for lowest GWP 
▪ 
NPC = Insulation thickness optimised for lowest NPC 
▪ 
AVG = Average insulation thickness of the two above 
scenarios 
o C# 
Case building 
o ZZ 
Heat supply 
▪ 
DH = District heating 
▪ 
EH = Direct electric heating 
▪ 
HP = Heat pump

G 
Multi-Object Optimisation 
The appendix shows the Python code used for Multi-Object Optimisation (MOO). The 
code is only shown for case 1 with district heating as heat supply. 
 
G.1 
Python Code (Case 1 with DH) 
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- 
!pip install pyomo 
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- 
!apt-get install -y -qq glpk-utils 
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- 
#Defining adjustment factor 
adjust_gwp = 0.988823 
adjust_npc = 1 - adjust_gwp 
 
#Assigning weight to GWP and NPC 
weight_gwp_c1_1 = 1.000 
weight_npc_c1_1 = 1 - weight_gwp_c1_1 
 
weight_gwp_c1_2 = 0.750 
weight_npc_c1_2 = 1 - weight_gwp_c1_2 
 
weight_gwp_c1_3 = 0.500 
weight_npc_c1_3 = 1 - weight_gwp_c1_3 
 
weight_gwp_c1_4 = 0.250 
weight_npc_c1_4 = 1 - weight_gwp_c1_4 
 
weight_gwp_c1_5 = 0.000 
weight_npc_c1_5 = 1 - weight_gwp_c1_5 
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- 
# GWP and NPC values for each variant of each component

    ('external_wall', 1): 23.82,

from pyomo.environ import * 
 
# Sample data: Change this according to your actual data 
==PAGE:165==
 
Appendix 
 
156 
 
 
 
 
components = ['external_wall', 'internal_wall', 'roof', 'ground_deck', 'floor_deck', 'win-
dow'] 
variants = range(1, 17)  # Assuming each component has 16 variants 
 
# area in square meters 
component_areas = { 
    'external_wall': 191.3, 
    'internal_wall': 141.9, 
    'roof': 213.0, 
    'ground_deck': 185.5, 
    'floor_deck': 0.0, 
    'window': 26.6, 
    # ... other components 
} 
 
#Assigning weight to GWP and NPC 
weight_gwp = adjust_gwp * weight_gwp_c1_1  # Adjust this value as needed 
weight_npc = adjust_npc * weight_npc_c1_1  # Adjust this value as needed 
 
# Storing original GWP and NPC values 
original_GWP = GWP.copy() 
original_NPC = NPC.copy() 
 
for key in GWP: 
    component, variant = key 
    GWP[key] *= component_areas[component] 
 
for key in NPC: 
    component, variant = key 
    NPC[key] *= component_areas[component] 
 
# Model 
model = ConcreteModel() 
 
# Decision variables: Whether to select a particular variant of a component 
model.x = Var(((c, v) for c in components for v in variants), within=Binary) 
 
def gwp_rule(model): 
    return sum(GWP[c, v] * model.x[c, v] for c in components for v in variants) 
 
def npc_rule(model): 
    return sum(NPC[c, v] * model.x[c, v] for c in components for v in variants) 
 
# Objective: Minimize GWP and NPC 
# Combined weighted objective 
def combined_objective_rule(model): 
    return weight_gwp * gwp_rule(model) + weight_npc * npc_rule(model) 
 
==PAGE:166==
 
Appendix 
 
157 
 
 
 
 
# Set the combined objective in the model 
model.CombinedObjective 
= 
Objective(rule=combined_objective_rule, 
sense=minimize) 
 
# Constraint: Only one variant of each component can be selected 
def component_rule(model, c): 
    return sum(model.x[c, v] for v in variants) == 1 
 
model.ComponentConstraint = Constraint(components, rule=component_rule) 
 
# Solver 
solver = SolverFactory('glpk') 
results = solver.solve(model, tee=True) 
 
# Output results 
for c in components: 
    for v in variants: 
        if model.x[c, v].value == 1: 
            print(f"Component {c}, Variant {v} selected") 
 
# Calculate and print the total GWP and NPC of the selected variants 
total_gwp_c1_1 = sum(GWP[c, v] * model.x[c, v].value for c in components for v in var-
iants) 
total_npc_c1_1 = sum(NPC[c, v] * model.x[c, v].value for c in components for v in vari-
ants) 
 
print(f"Total GWP, C1_1: {total_gwp_c1_1}") 
print(f"Total NPC, C1_1: {total_npc_c1_1}") 
 
for c in components: 
    for v in variants: 
        if model.x[c, v].value == 1: 
            selected_variants_c1_1.append((c, v)) 
 
# Revert GWP and NPC values to original 
GWP = original_GWP.copy() 
NPC = original_NPC.copy()
